<p><b>Islam</b> (<span class="nowrap"><span class="IPA nopopups noexcerpt"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/English" title="Help:IPA/English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="/ˈ/: primary stress follows">ˈ</span><span title="/ɪ/: 'i' in 'kit'">ɪ</span><span title="'s' in 'sigh'">s</span><span title="'l' in 'lie'">l</span><span title="/ɑː/: 'a' in 'father'">ɑː</span><span title="'m' in 'my'">m</span></span>/</a></span></span><sup id="cite_ref-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-1">[note 1]</a></sup>) is an <a href="/wiki/Abrahamic_religions" title="Abrahamic religions">Abrahamic</a> <a href="/wiki/Monotheism" title="Monotheism">monotheistic</a> religion teaching that there is only <a href="/wiki/Tawhid" title="Tawhid">one incomparable God</a> (<a href="/wiki/Allah" title="Allah">Allah</a>)<sup id="cite_ref-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-2">[1]</a></sup> and that <a href="/wiki/Muhammad" title="Muhammad">Muhammad</a> is the <a href="/wiki/Prophets_and_messengers_in_Islam" title="Prophets and messengers in Islam">messenger</a> of God.<sup id="cite_ref-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-3">[2]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-OEIW-allah_4-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-OEIW-allah-4">[3]</a></sup> It is the <a href="/wiki/Major_religious_groups#Largest_religions" title="Major religious groups">world's second-largest religion</a><sup id="cite_ref-landscape_5-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-landscape-5">[4]</a></sup> and the <a href="/wiki/Growth_of_religion" title="Growth of religion">fastest-growing major religion in the world</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-6">[5]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Lippman_7-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Lippman-7">[6]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-8">[7]</a></sup> with over 1.8&#160;billion followers or 24.1% of the global population,<sup id="cite_ref-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-9">[8]</a></sup> known as <a href="/wiki/Muslim" title="Muslim">Muslims</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-10">[9]</a></sup> Muslims make up a majority of the population in <a href="/wiki/Islam_by_country" title="Islam by country">50 countries</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-landscape_5-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-landscape-5">[4]</a></sup> Islam teaches that <a href="/wiki/God_in_Islam" title="God in Islam">God</a> is <a href="/wiki/Mercy#Islam" title="Mercy">merciful</a>, <a href="/wiki/Omnipotence" title="Omnipotence">all-powerful</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tawhid" title="Tawhid">unique</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-11">[10]</a></sup> and has guided mankind through <a href="/wiki/Prophets_and_messengers_in_Islam" title="Prophets and messengers in Islam">prophets</a>, <a href="/wiki/Islamic_holy_books" title="Islamic holy books">revealed scriptures</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ayah" title="Ayah">natural signs</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-OEIW-allah_4-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-OEIW-allah-4">[3]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-12">[11]</a></sup> The primary scriptures of Islam are the <a href="/wiki/Quran" title="Quran">Quran</a>, viewed by Muslims as the <a href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/verbatim" class="extiw" title="wikt:verbatim">verbatim</a> word of God, and the teachings and normative example (called the <i><a href="/wiki/Sunnah" title="Sunnah">sunnah</a></i>, composed of accounts called <i><a href="/wiki/Hadith" title="Hadith">hadith</a></i>) of Muhammad (<a href="/wiki/Circa" title="Circa"><abbr title="circa">c.</abbr></a> 570–8 June 632 CE).</p>
<p>Muslims believe that Islam is the complete and universal version of a <a href="/wiki/Fitra" title="Fitra">primordial faith</a> that was revealed many times before through prophets including <a href="/wiki/Adam_in_Islam" title="Adam in Islam">Adam</a>, <a href="/wiki/Abraham_in_Islam" title="Abraham in Islam">Abraham</a>, <a href="/wiki/Moses_in_Islam" title="Moses in Islam">Moses</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Jesus_in_Islam" title="Jesus in Islam">Jesus</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-People-of-the-Book_13-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-People-of-the-Book-13">[12]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-14">[13]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-15">[14]</a></sup> As for the Quran, Muslims consider it to be the unaltered and final revelation of God.<sup id="cite_ref-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-16">[15]</a></sup> Like other <a href="/wiki/Abrahamic_religions" title="Abrahamic religions">Abrahamic religions</a>, Islam also teaches a final judgment with the righteous rewarded <a href="/wiki/Jannah" title="Jannah">paradise</a> and unrighteous punished in <a href="/wiki/Jahannam" title="Jahannam">hell</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-17">[16]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-18">[17]</a></sup> Religious concepts and practices include the <a href="/wiki/Five_Pillars_of_Islam" title="Five Pillars of Islam">Five Pillars of Islam</a>, which are obligatory acts of worship, and following <a href="/wiki/Sharia" title="Sharia">Islamic law</a>, which touches on virtually every aspect of life and society, from <a href="/wiki/Islamic_banking_and_finance" title="Islamic banking and finance">banking</a> and <a href="/wiki/Zakat" title="Zakat">welfare</a> to <a href="/wiki/Women_in_Islam" title="Women in Islam">women</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Islamic_ethics#Environmentalism" title="Islamic ethics">environment</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-19">[18]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-20">[19]</a></sup> The cities of <a href="/wiki/Mecca" title="Mecca">Mecca</a>, <a href="/wiki/Medina" title="Medina">Medina</a> and <a href="/wiki/Jerusalem" title="Jerusalem">Jerusalem</a> are home to <a href="/wiki/Holiest_sites_in_Islam" title="Holiest sites in Islam">the three holiest sites in Islam</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Trofimov_21-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Trofimov-21">[20]</a></sup></p>
<p>Apart from the Muslim viewpoint,<sup id="cite_ref-Esposito1998_22-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Esposito1998-22">[21]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Esposito2002b_23-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Esposito2002b-23">[22]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Peters2003_24-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Peters2003-24">[23]</a></sup> Islam is believed to have originated in the early 7th century <a href="/wiki/Common_Era" title="Common Era">CE</a> in Mecca,<sup id="cite_ref-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-25">[24]</a></sup> and by the 8th century the <a href="/wiki/Caliphate" title="Caliphate">Islamic empire</a> extended from <a href="/wiki/Al-Andalus" title="Al-Andalus">Iberia</a> in the west to the <a href="/wiki/Indus_River" title="Indus River">Indus River</a> in the east. The <a href="/wiki/Islamic_Golden_Age" title="Islamic Golden Age">Islamic Golden Age</a> refers to the period traditionally dated from the 8th century to the 13th century when much of the historically <a href="/wiki/Muslim_world" title="Muslim world">Islamic world</a> was experiencing a <a href="/wiki/Science_in_the_medieval_Islamic_world" title="Science in the medieval Islamic world">scientific</a>, <a href="/wiki/History_of_Islamic_economics#Classical_Muslim_commerce" title="History of Islamic economics">economic</a> and cultural flourishing.<sup id="cite_ref-Saliba_26-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Saliba-26">[25]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-King_27-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-King-27">[26]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Hassan-Decline_28-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hassan-Decline-28">[27]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Spread_of_Islam" title="Spread of Islam">expansion</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Muslim_world" title="Muslim world">Muslim world</a> involved various <a href="/wiki/Caliphate" title="Caliphate">caliphates</a> and <a href="/wiki/List_of_Muslim_empires_and_dynasties" class="mw-redirect" title="List of Muslim empires and dynasties">empires</a>, traders and <a href="/wiki/Conversion_to_Islam" class="mw-redirect" title="Conversion to Islam">conversion to Islam</a> by <a href="/wiki/Islamic_missionary_activity" title="Islamic missionary activity">missionary activities</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-29" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-29">[28]</a></sup></p>
<p>Most Muslims are of one of two <a href="/wiki/Islamic_schools_and_branches" title="Islamic schools and branches">denominations</a>:<sup id="cite_ref-NYT-20160103_30-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NYT-20160103-30">[29]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-NYT-20160105-maps_31-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NYT-20160105-maps-31">[30]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Sunni_Islam" title="Sunni Islam">Sunni</a> (75–90%)<sup id="cite_ref-Sunni-eb_32-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Sunni-eb-32">[31]</a></sup> or <a href="/wiki/Shia_Islam" title="Shia Islam">Shia</a> (10–20%).<sup id="cite_ref-Shia_33-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Shia-33">[32]</a></sup> About 13% of Muslims live in <a href="/wiki/Indonesia" title="Indonesia">Indonesia</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-Miller_2009.2C_pp.8.2C17_34-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Miller_2009.2C_pp.8.2C17-34">[33]</a></sup> the largest Muslim-majority country, 31% in <a href="/wiki/Islam_in_South_Asia" title="Islam in South Asia">South Asia</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-35" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-35">[34]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-36" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-36">[35]</a></sup> the largest population of Muslims in the world,<sup id="cite_ref-37" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-37">[36]</a></sup> 23% in the <a href="/wiki/Religion_in_the_Middle_East#Islam" title="Religion in the Middle East">Middle East</a>-<a href="/wiki/Islam_in_Africa" title="Islam in Africa">North Africa</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-38" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-38">[37]</a></sup> where it is the dominant religion,<sup id="cite_ref-pewmuslim22_39-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-pewmuslim22-39">[38]</a></sup> and 15% in <a href="/wiki/Sub-Saharan_Africa" title="Sub-Saharan Africa">Sub-Saharan Africa</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-mgmpPRC_40-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-mgmpPRC-40">[39]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-pewmuslim32_41-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-pewmuslim32-41">[40]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-42" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-42">[41]</a></sup> Sizable <a href="/wiki/Islam_by_country" title="Islam by country">Muslim communities</a> are also found in the <a href="/wiki/Islam_in_the_Americas" title="Islam in the Americas">Americas</a>, <a href="/wiki/Caucasus" title="Caucasus">Caucasus</a>, <a href="/wiki/Islam_in_China" title="Islam in China">China</a>, <a href="/wiki/Islam_in_Europe" title="Islam in Europe">Europe</a>, <a href="/wiki/Horn_of_Africa#Religion" title="Horn of Africa">Horn of Africa</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mainland_Southeast_Asia#Prevalence" title="Mainland Southeast Asia">Mainland Southeast Asia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Islam_in_the_Philippines" title="Islam in the Philippines">Philippines</a>, <a href="/wiki/Islam_in_Russia" title="Islam in Russia">Russia</a> and <a href="/wiki/Swahili_coast" title="Swahili coast">Swahili coast</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-pewmuslim12_43-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-pewmuslim12-43">[42]</a></sup></p>
<p></p>

<p></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Etymology_and_meaning">Etymology and meaning</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Kaaba_at_night.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/10/Kaaba_at_night.jpg/250px-Kaaba_at_night.jpg" width="250" height="188" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/10/Kaaba_at_night.jpg/375px-Kaaba_at_night.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/10/Kaaba_at_night.jpg/500px-Kaaba_at_night.jpg 2x" data-file-width="960" data-file-height="720" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Kaaba_at_night.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The <a href="/wiki/Kaaba" title="Kaaba">Kaaba</a> in <a href="/wiki/Mecca" title="Mecca">Mecca</a> is the <a href="/wiki/Qibla" title="Qibla">direction of prayer</a> and destination of pilgrimage for <a href="/wiki/Muslim" title="Muslim">Muslims</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Islam (<a href="/wiki/Arabic_language" class="mw-redirect" title="Arabic language">Arabic</a>: <span lang="ar" dir="rtl" xml:lang="ar">الإسلام</span>‎‎, <small>IPA:&#160;</small><span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/Arabic" title="Help:IPA/Arabic">[alʔisˈlaːm]</a></span><small class="nowrap metadata">&#160;(<span class="unicode haudio"><span class="fn"><span style="white-space:nowrap"><a href="/wiki/File:Ar-al_islam.ogg" title="About this sound"><img alt="About this sound" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Loudspeaker.svg/11px-Loudspeaker.svg.png" width="11" height="11" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Loudspeaker.svg/17px-Loudspeaker.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Loudspeaker.svg/22px-Loudspeaker.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="20" data-file-height="20" /></a>&#160;</span><a href="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/e/e5/Ar-al_islam.ogg" class="internal" title="Ar-al islam.ogg">listen</a></span></span>)</small>) is a verbal noun originating from the <a href="/wiki/Semitic_root" title="Semitic root">triliteral root</a> <a href="/wiki/S-L-M" class="mw-redirect" title="S-L-M">S-L-M</a> which forms a large class of words mostly relating to concepts of wholeness, submission, safeness, and peace.<sup id="cite_ref-44" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-44">[43]</a></sup> In a religious context it means "voluntary submission to God".<sup id="cite_ref-45" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-45">[44]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-46" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-46">[45]</a></sup> <i><a href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%D8%A5%D8%B3%D9%84%D8%A7%D9%85#Arabic" class="extiw" title="wikt:إسلام">Islām</a></i> is the verbal noun of <a href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%D8%A3%D8%B3%D9%84%D9%85#Arabic" class="extiw" title="wikt:أسلم">Form IV</a> of the root, and means "submission" or "surrender". <i><a href="/wiki/Muslim" title="Muslim">Muslim</a></i>, the word for an adherent of Islam, is the <a href="/wiki/Active_participle" class="mw-redirect" title="Active participle">active participle</a> of the same verb form, and means "one who submits" or "one who surrenders". The word sometimes has distinct connotations in its various occurrences in the <a href="/wiki/Quran" title="Quran">Quran</a>. In some verses, there is stress on the quality of Islam as an internal state: "Whomsoever God desires to guide, He opens his heart to Islam."<sup id="cite_ref-MULTIPLE-REFERENCES_47-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-MULTIPLE-REFERENCES-47">[46]</a></sup> Other verses connect <i>Islām</i> and <i><a href="/wiki/D%C4%ABn" class="mw-redirect" title="Dīn">dīn</a></i> (usually translated as "religion"): "Today, I have perfected your religion (<i>dīn</i>) for you; I have completed My blessing upon you; I have approved Islam for your religion."<sup id="cite_ref-48" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-48">[47]</a></sup> Still others describe Islam as an action of returning to God—more than just a verbal affirmation of faith.<sup id="cite_ref-49" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-49">[48]</a></sup> In the <a href="/wiki/Hadith_of_Gabriel" title="Hadith of Gabriel">Hadith of Gabriel</a>, <i>islām</i> is presented as one part of a triad that also includes <i><a href="/wiki/Iman_(concept)" title="Iman (concept)">imān</a></i> (faith), and <i><a href="/wiki/Ihsan" title="Ihsan">ihsān</a></i> (excellence).<sup id="cite_ref-50" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-50">[49]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-51" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-51">[50]</a></sup></p>
<p>Islam was historically called Muhammadanism in <a href="/wiki/Anglophone" class="mw-redirect" title="Anglophone">Anglophone</a> societies. This term has fallen out of use and is sometimes said to be <a href="/wiki/Religious_offense" title="Religious offense">offensive</a> because it suggests that a human being rather than God is central to Muslims' religion, parallel to Jesus Christ in Christianity. Some authors, however, continue to use the term <i>Muhammadanism</i> as a <a href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/technical_term" class="extiw" title="wikt:technical term">technical term</a> for the religious system as opposed to the <a href="/wiki/Theological" class="mw-redirect" title="Theological">theological</a> concept of Islam that exists within that system.<sup id="cite_ref-52" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-52">[51]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Articles_of_faith">Articles of faith</span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Aqidah" title="Aqidah">Aqidah</a> and <a href="/wiki/Iman_(concept)" title="Iman (concept)">Iman</a></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Faith" title="Faith">Faith</a> (<a href="/wiki/Iman_(concept)" title="Iman (concept)">Iman</a>) in the Islamic <a href="/wiki/Creed" title="Creed">creed</a> (<a href="/wiki/Aqidah" title="Aqidah">Aqidah</a>) is often represented as the <a href="/wiki/Iman_(concept)#The_six_articles_of_the_Islamic_faith" title="Iman (concept)">six articles of faith</a>, notably spelled out in the <a href="/wiki/Hadith_of_Gabriel" title="Hadith of Gabriel">Hadith of Gabriel</a>.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Concept_of_God">Concept of God</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/God_in_Islam" title="God in Islam">God in Islam</a> and <a href="/wiki/Allah" title="Allah">Allah</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Istanbul,_Hagia_Sophia,_Allah.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/60/Istanbul%2C_Hagia_Sophia%2C_Allah.jpg/220px-Istanbul%2C_Hagia_Sophia%2C_Allah.jpg" width="220" height="216" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/60/Istanbul%2C_Hagia_Sophia%2C_Allah.jpg/330px-Istanbul%2C_Hagia_Sophia%2C_Allah.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/60/Istanbul%2C_Hagia_Sophia%2C_Allah.jpg/440px-Istanbul%2C_Hagia_Sophia%2C_Allah.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2118" data-file-height="2077" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Istanbul,_Hagia_Sophia,_Allah.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Medallion showing the word "<a href="/wiki/Allah" title="Allah">Allah</a>" (God) in <a href="/wiki/Hagia_Sophia" title="Hagia Sophia">Hagia Sophia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Istanbul" title="Istanbul">Istanbul</a>, Turkey.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Islam is often seen as having the simplest doctrines of the major religions.<sup id="cite_ref-Lippman_7-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Lippman-7">[6]</a></sup> Its most fundamental concept is a rigorous monotheism, called <a href="/wiki/Taw%E1%B8%A5%C4%ABd" class="mw-redirect" title="Tawḥīd">tawḥīd</a> (<a href="/wiki/Arabic_language" class="mw-redirect" title="Arabic language">Arabic</a>: <span lang="ar" dir="rtl" xml:lang="ar">توحيد</span>‎‎). God is described in chapter 112 of the Quran as: "Say, He is God, the One and Only; God, the Eternal, Absolute; He begetteth not, nor is He begotten; And there is none like unto Him" (<a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://www.perseus.tufts.edu/hopper/text?doc=Perseus%3Atext%3A2002.02.0006%3Asura%3D112%3Averse%3D1-4">112:1-4</a>).<sup id="cite_ref-53" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-53">[52]</a></sup> Muslims repudiate <a href="/wiki/Polytheism" title="Polytheism">polytheism</a> and <a href="/wiki/Idolatry" title="Idolatry">idolatry</a>, called <a href="/wiki/Shirk_(Islam)" title="Shirk (Islam)">Shirk</a>, and reject the Christian doctrine of the <a href="/wiki/Trinity" title="Trinity">Trinity</a> and divinity of <a href="/wiki/Islamic_view_of_Jesus" class="mw-redirect" title="Islamic view of Jesus">Jesus</a>. In Islam, God is beyond all comprehension and Muslims are not expected to visualize God.<sup id="cite_ref-54" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-54">[53]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-55" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-55">[54]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-56" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-56">[55]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-57" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-57">[56]</a></sup> God is described and referred to by certain names or attributes, the most common being <i>Al-Rahmān</i>, meaning "The Compassionate" and <i>Al-Rahīm</i>, meaning "The Merciful" (See <a href="/wiki/Names_of_God_in_Islam" title="Names of God in Islam">Names of God in Islam</a>).<sup id="cite_ref-Ben_58-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ben-58">[57]</a></sup></p>
<p>Muslims believe that the creation of everything in the universe was brought into being by God's sheer command, "'Be' and so it is,"<sup id="cite_ref-59" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-59">[58]</a></sup> and that the <a href="/wiki/Purpose_of_life" class="mw-redirect" title="Purpose of life">purpose of existence</a> is to worship God.<sup id="cite_ref-60" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-60">[59]</a></sup> He is viewed as a personal god who responds whenever a person in need or distress calls him.<sup id="cite_ref-61" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-61">[60]</a></sup> There are no intermediaries, such as clergy, to contact God who states, "I am nearer to him than (his) <a href="/wiki/Jugular_vein" title="Jugular vein">jugular vein</a>."<sup id="cite_ref-62" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-62">[61]</a></sup> God consciousness is referred to as <a href="/wiki/Taqwa" title="Taqwa">Taqwa</a>.</p>
<p><i><a href="/wiki/All%C4%81h" class="mw-redirect" title="Allāh">Allāh</a></i> is the term with no <a href="/wiki/Plural" title="Plural">plural</a> or <a href="/wiki/Gender" title="Gender">gender</a> used by Muslims and Arabic-speaking Christians and Jews to reference God, while <i><span title="ISO 233 Arabic" class="Unicode" style="white-space:normal; text-decoration: none"><a href="/wiki/%CA%BEil%C4%81h" class="mw-redirect" title="ʾilāh">ʾilāh</a></span></i> (<a href="/wiki/Arabic_language" class="mw-redirect" title="Arabic language">Arabic</a>: <span lang="ar" dir="rtl" xml:lang="ar">إله</span>‎‎) is the term used for a deity or a god in general.<sup id="cite_ref-63" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-63">[62]</a></sup> Other non-Arab Muslims might use different names as much as Allah, for instance "Tanrı" in <a href="/wiki/Turkish_language" title="Turkish language">Turkish</a>, "Khodā" in <a href="/wiki/Persian_language" title="Persian language">Persian</a> or <i><span title="Urdu ALA-LC transliteration" class="Unicode" style="white-space:normal; text-decoration: none">Ḵẖudā</span></i> in <a href="/wiki/Urdu" title="Urdu">Urdu</a>.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Angels">Angels</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Islamic_view_of_angels" title="Islamic view of angels">Islamic view of angels</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Israfil1.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/92/Israfil1.png/220px-Israfil1.png" width="220" height="189" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/9/92/Israfil1.png 1.5x" data-file-width="273" data-file-height="234" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Israfil1.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Islamic_calligraphy" title="Islamic calligraphy">Islamic calligraphy</a> of the Archangel <a href="/wiki/Israfil" title="Israfil">Israfil</a> (reflects upon how <a href="/wiki/Angel" title="Angel">angels</a> are most commonly represented in Islam).</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Belief in <a href="/wiki/Angel" title="Angel">angels</a> is fundamental to the faith of Islam. The Arabic word for angel (<a href="/wiki/Arabic_language" class="mw-redirect" title="Arabic language">Arabic</a>: <span lang="ar" dir="rtl" xml:lang="ar">ملك</span>‎‎ <i><span title="ar ALA transliteration" class="Unicode" style="white-space:normal; text-decoration: none">malak</span></i>) means "<a href="/wiki/Courier" title="Courier">messenger</a>", like its counterparts in <a href="/wiki/Hebrew" class="mw-redirect" title="Hebrew">Hebrew</a> (<i>malʾákh</i>) and <a href="/wiki/Greek_language" title="Greek language">Greek</a> (<i>angelos</i>). According to the <a href="/wiki/Quran" title="Quran">Quran</a>, <a href="/wiki/Angels" class="mw-redirect" title="Angels">angels</a> do not possess <a href="/wiki/Free_will" title="Free will">free will</a>, and therefore worship and obey God in total obedience. Angels' duties include communicating <a href="/wiki/Revelation" title="Revelation">revelations</a> from God, glorifying God, recording every person's actions, and taking a person's <a href="/wiki/Soul" title="Soul">soul</a> at the time of death. Muslims believe that angels are made of light. They are described as "messengers with wings—two, or three, or four (pairs): He [God] adds to Creation as He pleases..."<sup id="cite_ref-64" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-64">[63]</a></sup> Some scholars have emphasized a metaphorical reinterpretation of the concept of angels.<sup id="cite_ref-65" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-65">[64]</a></sup> Pictorial depictions of angels are generally avoided in Islamic art, as the idea of giving form to anything immaterial is not accepted.<sup id="cite_ref-Baksh2007_66-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Baksh2007-66">[65]</a></sup> Muslims therefore do not generally share the perceptions of angelic pictorial depictions, such as those found in Western art.</p>
<p>Additionally, another kind of being that is sapient in Islam is called <a href="/wiki/Jinn" title="Jinn">Jinn</a>, who are believed to be invisible to humans, including the <a href="/wiki/Devil_in_Islam" class="mw-redirect" title="Devil in Islam">Satans</a>.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Revelations">Revelations</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Quran" title="Quran">Quran</a>, <a href="/wiki/Wahy" title="Wahy">Wahy</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Islamic_holy_books" title="Islamic holy books">Islamic holy books</a></div>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/History_of_the_Quran" title="History of the Quran">History of the Quran</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:FirstSurahKoran_(fragment).jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/83/FirstSurahKoran_%28fragment%29.jpg/220px-FirstSurahKoran_%28fragment%29.jpg" width="220" height="252" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/83/FirstSurahKoran_%28fragment%29.jpg/330px-FirstSurahKoran_%28fragment%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/83/FirstSurahKoran_%28fragment%29.jpg/440px-FirstSurahKoran_%28fragment%29.jpg 2x" data-file-width="444" data-file-height="509" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:FirstSurahKoran_(fragment).jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The first <a href="/wiki/Sura" class="mw-redirect" title="Sura">chapter</a> of the Quran, <i><a href="/wiki/Al-Fatiha" title="Al-Fatiha">Al-Fatiha</a></i>, consisting of seven verses.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The Islamic holy books are the records which most Muslims believe were dictated by God to various prophets. <a href="/wiki/Muslim" title="Muslim">Muslims</a> believe that parts of the previously revealed scriptures, the <i><a href="/wiki/Tawrat" class="mw-redirect" title="Tawrat">Tawrat</a></i> (<a href="/wiki/Torah" title="Torah">Torah</a>) and the <i><a href="/wiki/Injil" class="mw-redirect" title="Injil">Injil</a></i> (<a href="/wiki/Gospels" class="mw-redirect" title="Gospels">Gospels</a>), had become <a href="/wiki/Tahrif" title="Tahrif">distorted</a>—either in interpretation, in text, or both.<sup id="cite_ref-Distorted_67-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Distorted-67">[66]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Quran" title="Quran">Quran</a> (literally, "Reading" or "Recitation") is viewed by Muslims as the final revelation and literal word of God and is widely regarded as the finest <a href="/wiki/Arabic_literature" title="Arabic literature">literary</a> work in the <a href="/wiki/Arabic_language" class="mw-redirect" title="Arabic language">Arabic language</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-68" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-68">[67]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-69" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-69">[68]</a></sup></p>
<p>Muslims believe that the verses of the Quran were revealed to <a href="/wiki/Muhammad" title="Muhammad">Muhammad</a> by God through the <a href="/wiki/Holy_Spirit_(Islam)" class="mw-redirect" title="Holy Spirit (Islam)">archangel Gabriel</a> (<i>Jibrīl</i>) on many occasions between 610 CE until his death on June 8, 632.<sup id="cite_ref-70" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-70">[69]</a></sup> While Muhammad was alive, all of these revelations were written down by his companions (<i><a href="/wiki/Sahabah" title="Sahabah">sahabah</a></i>), although the prime method of transmission was orally through <a href="/wiki/Hafiz_(Quran)" title="Hafiz (Quran)">memorization</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-al_faruqi_71-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-al_faruqi-71">[70]</a></sup></p>
<p>The Quran is divided into 114 <a href="/wiki/Sura" class="mw-redirect" title="Sura">suras</a>, or chapters, which combined, contain 6,236 <i><a href="/wiki/Ayah" title="Ayah">āyāt</a></i>, or verses. The chronologically earlier suras, revealed at <a href="/wiki/Mecca" title="Mecca">Mecca</a>, are primarily concerned with ethical and spiritual topics. The later <a href="/wiki/Medina" title="Medina">Medinan</a> suras mostly discuss social and moral issues relevant to the Muslim community.<sup id="cite_ref-72" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-72">[71]</a></sup></p>
<p>The Quran is more concerned with moral guidance than legal instruction, and is considered the "sourcebook of Islamic principles and values".<sup id="cite_ref-73" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-73">[72]</a></sup> Muslim jurists consult the <i>hadith</i> ("reports"), or the written record of Prophet Muhammad's life, to both supplement the Quran and assist with its interpretation. The science of Quranic commentary and exegesis is known as <i><a href="/wiki/Tafsir" title="Tafsir">tafsir</a></i>.<sup id="cite_ref-74" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-74">[73]</a></sup> The set of rules governing proper pronunciation is called <i><a href="/wiki/Tajwid" title="Tajwid">tajwid</a></i>.</p>
<p>Muslims usually view "the Quran" as the original scripture as revealed in Arabic and that any translations are necessarily deficient, which are regarded only as commentaries on the Quran.<sup id="cite_ref-75" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-75">[74]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Prophets_and_sunnah">Prophets and sunnah</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Prophets_in_Islam" class="mw-redirect" title="Prophets in Islam">Prophets in Islam</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sunnah" title="Sunnah">Sunnah</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Hadith" title="Hadith">Hadith</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Al-Anbiya.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1a/Al-Anbiya.png/220px-Al-Anbiya.png" width="220" height="166" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1a/Al-Anbiya.png/330px-Al-Anbiya.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1a/Al-Anbiya.png/440px-Al-Anbiya.png 2x" data-file-width="1518" data-file-height="1143" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Al-Anbiya.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The Arabic word for prophets preceded by the honorific "peace be upon them".</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Muslims identify the prophets of Islam (<a href="/wiki/Arabic_language" class="mw-redirect" title="Arabic language">Arabic</a>: <span lang="ar" dir="rtl" xml:lang="ar">أنۢبياء</span>‎‎ <i><span title="ar ALA transliteration" class="Unicode" style="white-space:normal; text-decoration: none">anbiyāʾ</span></i> ) as those humans chosen by God to be his messengers. According to the Quran, the prophets were instructed by God to bring the "will of God" to the peoples of the nations. Muslims believe that prophets are human and not divine, though some are able to perform miracles to prove their claim. <a href="/wiki/Islamic_theology" class="mw-redirect" title="Islamic theology">Islamic theology</a> says that all of God's messengers preached the message of Islam—submission to the will of God. The Quran mentions the names of numerous figures considered <a href="/wiki/Prophets_in_Islam" class="mw-redirect" title="Prophets in Islam">prophets in Islam</a>, including <a href="/wiki/Adam_(Bible)" class="mw-redirect" title="Adam (Bible)">Adam</a>, <a href="/wiki/Islamic_view_of_Noah" class="mw-redirect" title="Islamic view of Noah">Noah</a>, <a href="/wiki/Abraham" title="Abraham">Abraham</a>, <a href="/wiki/Islamic_view_of_Moses" class="mw-redirect" title="Islamic view of Moses">Moses</a> and <a href="/wiki/Jesus_in_Islam" title="Jesus in Islam">Jesus</a>, among others.<sup id="cite_ref-76" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-76">[75]</a></sup></p>
<p>Muslims believe that God finally sent Muhammad as the last law bearing prophet (<i><a href="/wiki/Seal_of_the_prophets" class="mw-redirect" title="Seal of the prophets">Seal of the prophets</a></i>) to convey the divine message to the whole world (to sum up and to finalize the word of God). In Islam, the "normative" example of Muhammad's life is called the <a href="/wiki/Sunnah" title="Sunnah">Sunnah</a> (literally "trodden path"). Muslims are encouraged to emulate Muhammad's actions in their daily lives and the Sunnah is seen as crucial to guiding interpretation of the Quran.<sup id="cite_ref-77" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-77">[76]</a></sup> This example is preserved in traditions known as hadith, which recount his words, his actions, and his personal characteristics. Hadith Qudsi is a sub-category of hadith, regarded as verbatim words of God quoted by Muhammad but is not part of the Quran.</p>
<p>A hadith involves two elements- a chain of narrators, called <a href="/wiki/Hadith_studies#Sanad_and_matn" title="Hadith studies">sanad</a>, and the actual wording, called <a href="/wiki/Hadith_studies#Sanad_and_matn" title="Hadith studies">matn</a>. Hadiths can be classified, by studying the narration, as "authentic" or "correct", called <i><a href="/wiki/Hadith_terminology#.E1.B9.A2a.E1.B8.A5.C4.AB.E1.B8.A5" title="Hadith terminology">Sahih</a></i> (<a href="/wiki/Arabic_language" class="mw-redirect" title="Arabic language">Arabic</a>: <span lang="ar" dir="rtl" xml:lang="ar">صَحِيْح</span>‎‎), "good", called <i><a href="/wiki/Hadith_terminology#.E1.B8.A4asan" title="Hadith terminology">Ḥasan</a></i> (<a href="/wiki/Arabic_language" class="mw-redirect" title="Arabic language">Arabic</a>: <span lang="ar" dir="rtl" xml:lang="ar">حَسَن</span>‎‎) or "weak", called <i><a href="/wiki/Hadith_terminology#.E1.B8.8Ca.CA.BB.C4.ABf" title="Hadith terminology">Ḍaʻīf</a></i> (<a href="/wiki/Arabic_language" class="mw-redirect" title="Arabic language">Arabic</a>: <span lang="ar" dir="rtl" xml:lang="ar">ضَعِيْف</span>‎‎) among others. <a href="/wiki/Muhammad_al-Bukhari" title="Muhammad al-Bukhari">Muhammad al-Bukhari</a><sup id="cite_ref-sahih-bukhari.com_78-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-sahih-bukhari.com-78">[77]</a></sup> collected over 300,000 hadith, but only included 2,602 distinct hadith that passed the tests that codified them as authentic into his book <a href="/wiki/Sahih_al-Bukhari" title="Sahih al-Bukhari">Sahih al-Bukhari</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-sahih-bukhari.com_78-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-sahih-bukhari.com-78">[77]</a></sup> which is considered by Sunnis to be the most <a href="/wiki/Hadith_terminology#Terminology_relating_to_the_authenticity_of_a_hadith" title="Hadith terminology">authentic</a> source after the Quran.<sup id="cite_ref-79" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-79">[78]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Muqaddimah_80-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Muqaddimah-80">[79]</a></sup> Another famous source(s) of hadiths is known as <a href="/wiki/The_Four_Books" title="The Four Books">The Four Books</a>, which Shias consider as the most authentic hadith reference.<sup id="cite_ref-Meri_81-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Meri-81">[80]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-82" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-82">[81]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-83" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-83">[82]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Resurrection_and_judgment">Resurrection and judgment</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Qiyama" class="mw-redirect" title="Qiyama">Qiyama</a></div>
<p>Belief in the "Day of Resurrection", <i><a href="/wiki/Qiyamah" class="mw-redirect" title="Qiyamah">Yawm al-Qiyāmah</a></i> (<a href="/wiki/Arabic_language" class="mw-redirect" title="Arabic language">Arabic</a>: <span lang="ar" dir="rtl" xml:lang="ar">يوم القيامة</span>‎‎) is also crucial for Muslims. They believe the time of <i>Qiyāmah</i> is preordained by God but unknown to man. The trials and <a href="/wiki/Tribulation" class="mw-redirect" title="Tribulation">tribulations</a> preceding and during the <i>Qiyāmah</i> are described in the Quran and the hadith, and also in the commentaries of <a href="/wiki/Ulama" title="Ulama">scholars</a>. The Quran emphasizes <a href="/wiki/Resurrection_of_the_Dead" class="mw-redirect" title="Resurrection of the Dead">bodily resurrection</a>, a break from the <a href="/wiki/Pre-Islamic_Arabia" title="Pre-Islamic Arabia">pre-Islamic Arabian</a> understanding of death.<sup id="cite_ref-84" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-84">[83]</a></sup></p>
<p>On Yawm al-Qiyāmah, Muslims believe all mankind will be judged on their good and bad deeds and consigned to <i><a href="/wiki/Jannah" title="Jannah">Jannah</a></i> (paradise) or <i><a href="/wiki/Jahannam" title="Jahannam">Jahannam</a></i> (hell). The Qurʼan in Surat al-Zalzalah describes this as, "So whoever does an atom's weight of good will see it (99:7) and whoever does an atom's weight of evil will see it (99:8)." The Qurʼan <a href="/wiki/Islamic_views_of_sin" class="mw-redirect" title="Islamic views of sin">lists several sins</a> that can condemn a person to <a href="/wiki/Hell" title="Hell">hell</a>, such as <a href="/wiki/Kafir" title="Kafir">disbelief</a> in God (<a href="/wiki/Arabic_language" class="mw-redirect" title="Arabic language">Arabic</a>: <span lang="ar" dir="rtl" xml:lang="ar">كفر</span>‎‎ <i><span title="ar ALA transliteration" class="Unicode" style="white-space:normal; text-decoration: none">kufr</span></i>), and dishonesty; however, the Qurʼan makes it clear God will forgive the <a href="/wiki/Islamic_views_on_sin" title="Islamic views on sin">sins</a> of those who repent if he so wills. Good deeds, such as charity, prayer and compassion towards animals,<sup id="cite_ref-85" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-85">[84]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-86" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-86">[85]</a></sup> will be rewarded with entry to heaven. Muslims view <a href="/wiki/Jannah" title="Jannah">heaven</a> as a place of joy and blessings, with Qurʼanic references describing its features and the physical pleasures to come. Mystical traditions in Islam place these heavenly delights in the context of an ecstatic awareness of God.<sup id="cite_ref-87" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-87">[86]</a></sup> <i>Yawm al-Qiyāmah</i> is also identified in the Quran as <i>Yawm ad-Dīn</i> (<a href="/wiki/Arabic_language" class="mw-redirect" title="Arabic language">Arabic</a>: <span lang="ar" dir="rtl" xml:lang="ar">يوم الدين</span>‎‎), "Day of Religion";<sup id="cite_ref-88" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-88">[87]</a></sup> <i>as-sāʿah</i> (<a href="/wiki/Arabic_language" class="mw-redirect" title="Arabic language">Arabic</a>: <span lang="ar" dir="rtl" xml:lang="ar">الساعة</span>‎‎), "the Last Hour";<sup id="cite_ref-89" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-89">[88]</a></sup> and <i>al-Qāriʿah</i> (<a href="/wiki/Arabic_language" class="mw-redirect" title="Arabic language">Arabic</a>: <span lang="ar" dir="rtl" xml:lang="ar">القارعة</span>‎‎), "The Clatterer".<sup id="cite_ref-90" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-90">[89]</a></sup></p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Islamic_eschatology" title="Islamic eschatology">Islamic apocalyptic literature</a> describing <a href="/wiki/Armageddon" title="Armageddon">Armageddon</a> is often known as <i><a href="/wiki/Fitna_(word)" title="Fitna (word)">fitna</a></i> or <i><a href="/wiki/Al-Malhama_Al-Kubra" title="Al-Malhama Al-Kubra">malahim</a></i>. The <a href="/wiki/Mahdi" title="Mahdi">Mahdi</a> (prophesied redeemer) will be sent and with the help of <a href="/wiki/Jesus_in_Islam" title="Jesus in Islam">Jesus</a>, will battle <a href="/wiki/Al-Masih_ad-Dajjal" title="Al-Masih ad-Dajjal">the Antichrist</a>. They will triumph, liberating Islam from cruelty, and this will be followed by a time of serenity with people living true to religious values.<sup id="cite_ref-Mahdi1_91-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mahdi1-91">[90]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Divine_will">Divine will</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Qadar" class="mw-redirect" title="Qadar">Qadar</a></div>
<p>The concept of divine will is referred to as <i>al-qadā wa'l-qadar</i> (<a href="/wiki/Arabic_language" class="mw-redirect" title="Arabic language">Arabic</a>: <span lang="ar" dir="rtl" xml:lang="ar">قدر</span>‎‎), which literally derives from a root that means <i>to measure</i>. Everything, good and bad, is believed to have been decreed.<sup id="cite_ref-92" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-92">[91]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Acts_of_worship">Acts of worship</span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Five_Pillars_of_Islam" title="Five Pillars of Islam">Five Pillars of Islam</a></div>
<p>There are five basic religious acts in Islam, collectively known as 'The Pillars of Islam' (<i>arkan al-Islam</i>; also <i>arkan ad-din</i>, "pillars of religion"), which are considered obligatory for all believers. The Quran presents them as a framework for worship and a sign of commitment to the faith. They are (1) the creed (<a href="/wiki/Shahadah" class="mw-redirect" title="Shahadah">shahadah</a>), (2) daily prayers (<a href="/wiki/Salat" class="mw-redirect" title="Salat">salat</a>), (3) almsgiving (<a href="/wiki/Zakah" class="mw-redirect" title="Zakah">zakah</a>), (4) fasting during <a href="/wiki/Ramadan" title="Ramadan">Ramadan</a>, and (5) the pilgrimage to Mecca (<a href="/wiki/Hajj" title="Hajj">hajj</a>) at least once in a lifetime.<sup id="cite_ref-93" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-93">[92]</a></sup> Both <a href="/wiki/Shia_Islam" title="Shia Islam">Shia</a> and <a href="/wiki/Sunni_Islam" title="Sunni Islam">Sunni</a> sects agree on the essential details for the performance of these acts.<sup id="cite_ref-94" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-94">[93]</a></sup> Apart from these, Muslims also perform other religious acts. Notable among them are charity (<a href="/wiki/Sadaqah" title="Sadaqah">Sadaqah</a>) and <a href="/wiki/Quran_reading" title="Quran reading">recitation of the Quran</a>.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Testimony">Testimony</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Shahadah" class="mw-redirect" title="Shahadah">Shahadah</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Silver_Rupee_Akbar.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/88/Silver_Rupee_Akbar.jpg/220px-Silver_Rupee_Akbar.jpg" width="220" height="112" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/88/Silver_Rupee_Akbar.jpg/330px-Silver_Rupee_Akbar.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/88/Silver_Rupee_Akbar.jpg/440px-Silver_Rupee_Akbar.jpg 2x" data-file-width="588" data-file-height="300" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Silver_Rupee_Akbar.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Silver coin of the <a href="/wiki/Mughal_Emperor" class="mw-redirect" title="Mughal Emperor">Mughal Emperor</a> <a href="/wiki/Akbar" title="Akbar">Akbar</a> with inscriptions of the Islamic declaration of faith</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Shahadah" class="mw-redirect" title="Shahadah">Shahadah</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-95" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-95">[94]</a></sup> which is the basic <a href="/wiki/Creed" title="Creed">creed</a> of Islam that must be recited under <a href="/wiki/Oath" title="Oath">oath</a> with the specific statement: "<i><span title="ISO 233 Arabic" class="Unicode" style="white-space:normal; text-decoration: none">'ašhadu 'al-lā ilāha illā-llāhu wa 'ašhadu 'anna muħammadan rasūlu-llāh</span></i>", or "I testify that there is no god but <a href="/wiki/God_in_Islam" title="God in Islam">God</a>, Muhammad is the messenger of God."<sup id="cite_ref-96" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-96">[95]</a></sup> This testament is a foundation for all other beliefs and practices in Islam. Muslims must repeat the <i>shahadah</i> in prayer, and non-Muslims wishing to <a href="/wiki/Convert_to_Islam" class="mw-redirect" title="Convert to Islam">convert to Islam</a> are required to recite the creed.<sup id="cite_ref-97" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-97">[96]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Prayer">Prayer</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Salat" class="mw-redirect" title="Salat">Salat</a></div>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Mosque" title="Mosque">Mosque</a> and <a href="/wiki/Jumu%27ah" title="Jumu'ah">Jumu'ah</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Mosque.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e5/Mosque.jpg/220px-Mosque.jpg" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e5/Mosque.jpg/330px-Mosque.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e5/Mosque.jpg/440px-Mosque.jpg 2x" data-file-width="640" data-file-height="480" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Mosque.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Muslim men <a href="/wiki/Prostration" title="Prostration">prostrating</a> during prayer in the <a href="/wiki/Umayyad_Mosque" title="Umayyad Mosque">Umayyad Mosque</a>, <a href="/wiki/Damascus" title="Damascus">Damascus</a>.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Ritual prayers are called Ṣalāh or Ṣalāt (<a href="/wiki/Arabic_language" class="mw-redirect" title="Arabic language">Arabic</a>: <a href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%D8%B5%D9%84%D8%A7%D8%A9" class="extiw" title="wikt:صلاة">صلاة</a>). Salat is intended to focus the mind on God, and is seen as a personal communication with him that expresses gratitude and <a href="/wiki/Worship" title="Worship">worship</a>. Performing prayers five times a day is compulsory but flexibility in the specifics is allowed depending on circumstances. The prayers are recited in the <a href="/wiki/Arabic_language" class="mw-redirect" title="Arabic language">Arabic language</a>, and consist of verses from the Quran.<sup id="cite_ref-98" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-98">[97]</a></sup> The prayers are done with the chest in direction of the <a href="/wiki/Kaaba" title="Kaaba">kaaba</a> though in the early days of Islam, they were done in direction of <a href="/wiki/Jerusalem" title="Jerusalem">Jerusalem</a>. The act of supplicating is referred to as <a href="/wiki/Dua" title="Dua">dua</a>.</p>
<p>A mosque is a <a href="/wiki/Places_of_worship" class="mw-redirect" title="Places of worship">place of worship</a> for Muslims, who often refer to it by its Arabic name <i>masjid</i>. A large mosque for gathering for Friday prayers or Eid prayers are called <a href="/wiki/Jama_Masjid" title="Jama Masjid">masjid jāmi</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-99" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-99">[98]</a></sup> Although the primary purpose of the mosque is to serve as a place of prayer, it is also important to the <a href="/wiki/Ummah" title="Ummah">Muslim community</a> as a place to meet and study. In Medina, <a href="/wiki/Al-Masjid_al-Nabawi" class="mw-redirect" title="Al-Masjid al-Nabawi">Al-Masjid al-Nabawi</a>, or the Prophet's Mosque, was also a place of refuge for the poor.<sup id="cite_ref-100" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-100">[99]</a></sup> Modern mosques have evolved greatly from the early designs of the 7th century, and contain a variety of architectural elements such as <a href="/wiki/Minaret" title="Minaret">minarets</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-101" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-101">[100]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Charity">Charity</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Zakat" title="Zakat">Zakat</a> and <a href="/wiki/Sadaqah" title="Sadaqah">Sadaqah</a></div>
<p>"Zakāt" (<a href="/wiki/Arabic_language" class="mw-redirect" title="Arabic language">Arabic</a>: <span lang="ar" dir="rtl" xml:lang="ar">زكاة</span>‎‎ <i><span title="ar ALA transliteration" class="Unicode" style="white-space:normal; text-decoration: none">zakāh</span></i> "<a href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/alms" class="extiw" title="wikt:alms">alms</a>") is giving a fixed portion of accumulated wealth by those who can afford it to help the poor or needy and for those employed to collect Zakat; also, for bringing hearts together, freeing captives, for those in debt (or <a href="/wiki/Bonded_labour" class="mw-redirect" title="Bonded labour">bonded labour</a>) and for the (stranded) traveller.<sup id="cite_ref-102" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-102">[101]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-103" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-103">[102]</a></sup> It is considered a religious obligation (as opposed to voluntary charity) that the well-off owe to the needy because their wealth is seen as a "trust from God's bounty". Conservative estimates of annual zakat is estimated to be 15 times global humanitarian aid contributions.<sup id="cite_ref-104" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-104">[103]</a></sup> The amount of zakat to be paid on <a href="/wiki/Financial_capital" title="Financial capital">capital assets</a> (e.g. money) is 2.5% (1/40) per year,<sup id="cite_ref-AhmedGianci_105-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AhmedGianci-105">[104]</a></sup> for people who are not poor.</p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Sadaqah" title="Sadaqah">Sadaqah</a> means optional charity which is practiced as religious duty and out of generosity.<sup id="cite_ref-106" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-106">[105]</a></sup> Both the Quran and the hadith have put much emphasis on spending money for the welfare of needy people,<sup id="cite_ref-107" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-107">[106]</a></sup> and have urged the Muslims to give more as an act of optional charity.<sup id="cite_ref-108" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-108">[107]</a></sup> The Quran says: Spend something (in charity) out of the substance which We have bestowed on you, before Death should come to any of you (<a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://www.perseus.tufts.edu/hopper/text?doc=Perseus%3Atext%3A2002.02.0006%3Asura%3D63%3Averse%3D10">63:10</a>). One of the early teachings of Muhammad was that <a href="/wiki/God_in_Islam" title="God in Islam">God</a> expects men to be generous with their wealth and not to be miserly (Quran <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://www.perseus.tufts.edu/hopper/text?doc=Perseus%3Atext%3A2002.02.0006%3Asura%3D107">%3Averse%3D1 107&#160;:1–7</a>).<sup id="cite_ref-109" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-109">[108]</a></sup> Accumulating wealth without spending it to address the needs of the poor is generally prohibited and admonished.<sup id="cite_ref-110" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-110">[109]</a></sup> Another kind of charity in Islam is <a href="/wiki/Waqf" title="Waqf">waqf</a> which means perpetual religious endowment.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Fasting">Fasting</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Sawm" class="mw-redirect" title="Sawm">Sawm</a></div>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Sawm_of_Ramadan" class="mw-redirect" title="Sawm of Ramadan">Sawm of Ramadan</a></div>
<p>Fasting (<a href="/wiki/Arabic_language" class="mw-redirect" title="Arabic language">Arabic</a>: <span lang="ar" dir="rtl" xml:lang="ar">صوم</span>‎‎ <i><span title="ar ALA transliteration" class="Unicode" style="white-space:normal; text-decoration: none">ṣawm</span></i>) from food and drink, among other things, must be performed from dawn to dusk during the month of <a href="/wiki/Ramadan" title="Ramadan">Ramadan</a>. The fast is to encourage a feeling of nearness to God, and during it Muslims should express their gratitude for and dependence on him, atone for their past sins, and think of the needy. <i>Sawm</i> is not obligatory for several groups for whom it would constitute an undue burden. For others, flexibility is allowed depending on circumstances, but missed fasts usually must be made up quickly.<sup id="cite_ref-111" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-111">[110]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Pilgrimage">Pilgrimage</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Hajj" title="Hajj">Hajj</a> and <a href="/wiki/Umrah" title="Umrah">Umrah</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Al-Haram_mosque_-_Flickr_-_Al_Jazeera_English.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/92/Al-Haram_mosque_-_Flickr_-_Al_Jazeera_English.jpg/220px-Al-Haram_mosque_-_Flickr_-_Al_Jazeera_English.jpg" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/92/Al-Haram_mosque_-_Flickr_-_Al_Jazeera_English.jpg/330px-Al-Haram_mosque_-_Flickr_-_Al_Jazeera_English.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/92/Al-Haram_mosque_-_Flickr_-_Al_Jazeera_English.jpg/440px-Al-Haram_mosque_-_Flickr_-_Al_Jazeera_English.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3264" data-file-height="2448" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Al-Haram_mosque_-_Flickr_-_Al_Jazeera_English.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Pilgrims at the <a href="/wiki/Masjid_al-Haram" class="mw-redirect" title="Masjid al-Haram">Masjid al-Haram</a> in <a href="/wiki/Mecca" title="Mecca">Mecca</a> during <a href="/wiki/Hajj" title="Hajj">Hajj</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The obligatory Islamic <a href="/wiki/Pilgrimage" title="Pilgrimage">pilgrimage</a>, called the <i><span title="ar ALA transliteration" class="Unicode" style="white-space:normal; text-decoration: none">ḥajj</span></i> (<a href="/wiki/Arabic_language" class="mw-redirect" title="Arabic language">Arabic</a>: <span lang="ar" dir="rtl" xml:lang="ar">حج</span>‎‎), has to be performed during the <a href="/wiki/Islamic_calendar" title="Islamic calendar">Islamic month</a> of <i><a href="/wiki/Dhu_al-Hijjah" title="Dhu al-Hijjah">Dhu al-Hijjah</a></i> in the city of Mecca. Every able-bodied Muslim who can afford it must make the pilgrimage to Mecca at least once in his or her lifetime. Rituals of the Hajj include: spending a day and a night in the tents in the desert plain of Mina, then a day in the desert plain of Arafat praying and worshiping God, following the foot steps of Abraham; then spending a night out in the open, sleeping on the desert sand in the desert plain of Muzdalifah; then moving to Jamarat, symbolically <a href="/wiki/Stoning_of_the_Devil" title="Stoning of the Devil">stoning the Devil</a> recounting Abraham's actions;<sup id="cite_ref-112" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-112">[111]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-113" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-113">[112]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-114" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-114">[113]</a></sup> then going to Mecca and walking seven times around the <a href="/wiki/Kaaba" title="Kaaba">Kaaba</a> which Muslims believe was built as a place of worship by Abraham; then walking seven times between <a href="/wiki/Al-Safa_and_Al-Marwah" class="mw-redirect" title="Al-Safa and Al-Marwah">Mount Safa</a> and <a href="/wiki/Al-Safa_and_Al-Marwah" class="mw-redirect" title="Al-Safa and Al-Marwah">Mount Marwah</a> recounting the steps of Abraham's wife, while she was looking for water for her son Ismael in the desert before Mecca developed into a settlement.<sup id="cite_ref-115" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-115">[114]</a></sup> Another form of pilgrimage, <a href="/wiki/Umrah" title="Umrah">Umrah</a>, can be undertaken at any time of the year.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Recitation_and_memorization_of_the_Quran">Recitation and memorization of the Quran</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Men_reading_the_Koran_in_Umayyad_Mosque,_Damascus,_Syria.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/56/Men_reading_the_Koran_in_Umayyad_Mosque%2C_Damascus%2C_Syria.jpg/220px-Men_reading_the_Koran_in_Umayyad_Mosque%2C_Damascus%2C_Syria.jpg" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/56/Men_reading_the_Koran_in_Umayyad_Mosque%2C_Damascus%2C_Syria.jpg/330px-Men_reading_the_Koran_in_Umayyad_Mosque%2C_Damascus%2C_Syria.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/56/Men_reading_the_Koran_in_Umayyad_Mosque%2C_Damascus%2C_Syria.jpg/440px-Men_reading_the_Koran_in_Umayyad_Mosque%2C_Damascus%2C_Syria.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2813" data-file-height="1875" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Men_reading_the_Koran_in_Umayyad_Mosque,_Damascus,_Syria.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Men reading the Quran in a mosque</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Muslims recite and memorize the whole or part of the Quran as acts of virtue. <a href="/wiki/Tajwid" title="Tajwid">Reciting the Quran in the correct manner</a> has been described as an excellent act of worship.<sup id="cite_ref-Nigosian-70_116-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Nigosian-70-116">[115]</a></sup> Pious Muslims recite the whole Quran at the month of <a href="/wiki/Ramadan" title="Ramadan">Ramadan</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Stefon-42_117-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Stefon-42-117">[116]</a></sup> In Islamic societies, any social program generally begins with the recitation of the Quran.<sup id="cite_ref-Stefon-42_117-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Stefon-42-117">[116]</a></sup> One who has memorized the whole Quran is called a <i><a href="/wiki/Hafiz_(Quran)" title="Hafiz (Quran)">hafiz</a></i> who, it is said, will be able to intercede for ten people on the Last Judgment Day.<sup id="cite_ref-Nigosian-70_116-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Nigosian-70-116">[115]</a></sup> Apart from this, almost every Muslim memorizes some portion of the Quran because they need to recite it during their prayers.</p>
<div style="clear:both;"></div>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Law_and_jurisprudence">Law and jurisprudence</span></h2>
<table class="vertical-navbox nowraplinks" style="float:right;clear:right;width:22.0em;margin:0 0 1.0em 1.0em;background:#f9f9f9;border:1px solid #aaa;padding:0.2em;border-spacing:0.4em 0;text-align:center;line-height:1.4em;font-size:88%">
<tr>
<td style="padding-top:0.4em;line-height:1.2em">Part of <a href="/wiki/Category:Fiqh" title="Category:Fiqh">a series</a> on</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<th style="padding:0.2em 0.4em 0.2em;padding-top:0;font-size:145%;line-height:1.2em;background:lavender;padding-top:0.2em; display:block;margin-bottom:0.25em;"><a href="/wiki/Fiqh" title="Fiqh">Islamic jurisprudence<br />
(<i>fiqh</i>)</a></th>
</tr>
<tr>
<td style="padding:0 0.1em 0.4em">
<div class="NavFrame collapsed" style="border:none;padding:0">
<div class="NavHead" style="font-size:105%;background:transparent;text-align:left;background:lavender;padding-left:0.4em;text-align:center;padding-right:0.4em;"><a href="/wiki/Ibadah" title="Ibadah">Ritual</a></div>
<div class="NavContent plainlist" style="font-size:105%;padding:0.2em 0 0.4em;text-align:center;padding-left:1.5em;padding-right:1.5em;">
