A panoramic view of <a href="/wiki/Al-Masjid_al-Nabawi" class="mw-redirect" title="Al-Masjid al-Nabawi">Al-Masjid al-Nabawi</a> (the Mosque of the Prophet) in <a href="/wiki/Medina" title="Medina">Medina</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hejaz" title="Hejaz">Hejaz</a> region, today's Saudi Arabia, the second most sacred Mosque in Islam</div>
</div>
</div>
<h3><span id="Muhammad_(610–632)"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Muhammad_.28610.E2.80.93632.29">Muhammad (610–632)</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Muhammad" title="Muhammad">Muhammad</a> and <a href="/wiki/Muhammad_in_Islam" title="Muhammad in Islam">Muhammad in Islam</a></div>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Early_social_changes_under_Islam" title="Early social changes under Islam">Early social changes under Islam</a></div>
<p>Muslim tradition views Muhammad (c. 570 – June 8, 632) as the <a href="/wiki/Seal_of_the_prophets" class="mw-redirect" title="Seal of the prophets">seal of the prophets</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-180" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-180">[178]</a></sup> During the last 22 years of his life, beginning at age 40 in 610 <a href="/wiki/Common_Era" title="Common Era">CE</a>, according to the earliest surviving biographies, Muhammad reported revelations that he believed to be from God, conveyed to him through the <a href="/wiki/Holy_Spirit_(Islam)" class="mw-redirect" title="Holy Spirit (Islam)">archangel Gabriel</a> (<i>Jibril</i>). Muhammad's <a href="/wiki/Sahaba" class="mw-redirect" title="Sahaba">companions</a> memorized and recorded the content of these revelations, known as the Quran.<sup id="cite_ref-181" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-181">[179]</a></sup></p>
<p>During this time, <a href="/wiki/Muhammad_in_Mecca" title="Muhammad in Mecca">Muhammad in Mecca</a> preached to the people, imploring them to abandon polytheism and to worship one God. Although some converted to Islam, the leading Meccan authorities persecuted Muhammad and his followers. This resulted in the <a href="/wiki/Migration_to_Abyssinia" title="Migration to Abyssinia">Migration to Abyssinia</a> of some Muslims (to the <a href="/wiki/Aksumite_Empire" class="mw-redirect" title="Aksumite Empire">Aksumite Empire</a>). Many early converts to Islam were the poor and former slaves like <a href="/wiki/Bilal_ibn_Rabah_al-Habashi" class="mw-redirect" title="Bilal ibn Rabah al-Habashi">Bilal ibn Rabah al-Habashi</a>. The Meccan élite felt that Muhammad was destabilising their social order by preaching about one God and about racial equality, and that in the process he gave ideas to the poor and to their slaves.<sup id="cite_ref-182" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-182">[180]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-183" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-183">[181]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-184" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-184">[182]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-185" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-185">[183]</a></sup></p>
<p>After 12 years of the <a href="/wiki/Persecution_of_Muslims_by_the_Meccans" class="mw-redirect" title="Persecution of Muslims by the Meccans">persecution of Muslims by the Meccans</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Meccan_boycott_of_the_Hashemites" title="Meccan boycott of the Hashemites">Meccan boycott of the Hashemites</a>, Muhammad's relatives, Muhammad and the Muslims performed the <i><a href="/wiki/Hijra_(Islam)" class="mw-redirect" title="Hijra (Islam)">Hijra</a></i> ("emigration") to the city of <a href="/wiki/Medina" title="Medina">Medina</a> (formerly known as <i>Yathrib</i>) in 622. There, with the Medinan converts (<i><a href="/wiki/Ansar_(Islam)" title="Ansar (Islam)">Ansar</a></i>) and the Meccan migrants (<i><a href="/wiki/Muhajirun" title="Muhajirun">Muhajirun</a></i>), <a href="/wiki/Muhammad_in_Medina" title="Muhammad in Medina">Muhammad in Medina</a> established his political and <a href="/wiki/Theocracy" title="Theocracy">religious authority</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Constitution_of_Medina" title="Constitution of Medina">Constitution of Medina</a> was formulated, instituting a number of rights and responsibilities for the Muslim, Jewish, Christian and pagan communities of Medina, bringing them within the fold of one community—the <a href="/wiki/Ummah" title="Ummah">Ummah</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-186" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-186">[184]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-187" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-187">[185]</a></sup></p>
<p>The Constitution established:</p>
<ul>
<li>the security of the community</li>
<li>religious freedoms</li>
<li>the role of Medina as a sacred place (barring all violence and weapons)</li>
<li>the security of women</li>
<li>stable tribal relations within Medina</li>
<li>a tax system for supporting the community in time of conflict</li>
<li>parameters for exogenous political alliances</li>
<li>a system for granting protection of individuals</li>
<li>a judicial system for resolving disputes where non-Muslims could also use their own laws and have their own judges.<sup id="cite_ref-B._Serjeant_1978_188-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-B._Serjeant_1978-188">[186]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Watt_1964_p.4_189-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Watt_1964_p.4-189">[187]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Constitution_of_Medina_190-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Constitution_of_Medina-190">[188]</a></sup></li>
</ul>
<p>All the tribes signed the agreement to defend Medina from all external threats and to live in harmony amongst themselves. Within a few years, two battles took place against the Meccan forces: first, the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Badr" title="Battle of Badr">Battle of Badr</a> in 624 – a Muslim victory, and then a year later, when the Meccans returned to Medina, the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Uhud" title="Battle of Uhud">Battle of Uhud</a>, which ended inconclusively.</p>
<p>The Arab tribes in the rest of Arabia then formed a confederation and during the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_the_Trench" title="Battle of the Trench">Battle of the Trench</a> (March–April 627) besieged Medina, intent on finishing off Islam. In 628, the <a href="/wiki/Treaty_of_Hudaybiyyah" title="Treaty of Hudaybiyyah">Treaty of Hudaybiyyah</a> was signed between Mecca and the Muslims and was broken by Mecca two years later. After the signing of the Treaty of Hudaybiyyah many more people converted to Islam. At the same time, Meccan trade routes were cut off as Muhammad brought surrounding desert tribes under his control.<sup id="cite_ref-191" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-191">[189]</a></sup> By 629 Muhammad was victorious in the nearly bloodless <a href="/wiki/Conquest_of_Mecca" title="Conquest of Mecca">conquest of Mecca</a>, and by the time of his death in 632 (at the age of 62) he had united the <a href="/wiki/Tribes_of_Arabia" title="Tribes of Arabia">tribes of Arabia</a> into a single religious <a href="/wiki/Polity" title="Polity">polity</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-EoI-Muhammad_192-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-EoI-Muhammad-192">[190]</a></sup></p>
<p>The earliest three generations of Muslims are known as the <a href="/wiki/Salaf" title="Salaf">Salaf</a>, with the companions of Muhammad being known as the <a href="/wiki/Sahaba" class="mw-redirect" title="Sahaba">Sahaba</a>. Many of them, such as the largest narrator of hadith <a href="/wiki/Abu_Hureyrah" class="mw-redirect" title="Abu Hureyrah">Abu Hureyrah</a>, recorded and compiled what would constitute the sunnah.</p>
<h3><span id="Caliphate_and_civil_strife_(632–750)"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Caliphate_and_civil_strife_.28632.E2.80.93750.29">Caliphate and civil strife (632–750)</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Muslim_conquests" class="mw-redirect" title="Muslim conquests">Muslim conquests</a>, <a href="/wiki/First_Fitna" title="First Fitna">First Fitna</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Second_Fitna" title="Second Fitna">Second Fitna</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Dome_of_the_Rock1.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/14/Dome_of_the_Rock1.jpg/220px-Dome_of_the_Rock1.jpg" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/14/Dome_of_the_Rock1.jpg/330px-Dome_of_the_Rock1.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/14/Dome_of_the_Rock1.jpg/440px-Dome_of_the_Rock1.jpg 2x" data-file-width="640" data-file-height="480" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Dome_of_the_Rock1.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Dome_of_the_Rock" title="Dome of the Rock">Dome of the Rock</a> built by <a href="/wiki/Abd_al-Malik_ibn_Marwan" title="Abd al-Malik ibn Marwan">Abd al-Malik ibn Marwan</a>; completed at the end of the <a href="/wiki/Second_Fitna" title="Second Fitna">Second Fitna</a>.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>With Muhammad's death in 632, disagreement broke out over who would succeed him as leader of the Muslim community. <a href="/wiki/Abu_Bakr" title="Abu Bakr">Abu Bakr</a>, a companion and close friend of Muhammad, was made the first <a href="/wiki/Caliph" class="mw-redirect" title="Caliph">caliph</a>. Under Abu Bakr, Muslims put down a rebellion by Arab tribes in an episode known as the <a href="/wiki/Ridda_wars" title="Ridda wars">Ridda wars</a>, or "Wars of Apostasy".<sup id="cite_ref-193" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-193">[191]</a></sup> The Quran was compiled into a single volume at this time.</p>
<p>Abu Bakr's death in 634 resulted in the succession of <a href="/wiki/Umar_ibn_al-Khattab" class="mw-redirect" title="Umar ibn al-Khattab">Umar ibn al-Khattab</a> as the caliph, followed by <a href="/wiki/Uthman_ibn_al-Affan" class="mw-redirect" title="Uthman ibn al-Affan">Uthman ibn al-Affan</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ali_ibn_Abi_Talib" class="mw-redirect" title="Ali ibn Abi Talib">Ali ibn Abi Talib</a> and <a href="/wiki/Hasan_ibn_Ali" title="Hasan ibn Ali">Hasan ibn Ali</a>. The first four caliphs are known in Sunni Islam as <i>al-khulafā' ar-rāshidūn</i> ("<a href="/wiki/Rightly_Guided_Caliphs" class="mw-redirect" title="Rightly Guided Caliphs">Rightly Guided Caliphs</a>").<sup id="cite_ref-194" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-194">[192]</a></sup> Under them, the territory under Muslim rule expanded deeply into the parts of the <a href="/wiki/Sassanid_Empire" class="mw-redirect" title="Sassanid Empire">Persian</a> and <a href="/wiki/Byzantine" class="mw-redirect" title="Byzantine">Byzantine</a> territories.<sup id="cite_ref-195" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-195">[193]</a></sup></p>
<p>When Umar was assassinated by Persians in 644, <a href="/wiki/The_election_of_Uthman" class="mw-redirect" title="The election of Uthman">the election of Uthman</a> as successor was met with increasing opposition. The standard copies of the Quran were also distributed throughout the Islamic State. In 656, Uthman was also killed, and <a href="/wiki/Ali_ibn_Abi_Talib" class="mw-redirect" title="Ali ibn Abi Talib">Ali</a> assumed the position of caliph. This led to the <a href="/wiki/First_Fitna" title="First Fitna">first civil war</a> (the "First Fitna") over who should be caliph. Ali was assassinated by <a href="/wiki/Kharijites" class="mw-redirect" title="Kharijites">Kharijites</a> in 661. To avoid further fighting, the new caliph <a href="/wiki/Hasan_ibn_Ali" title="Hasan ibn Ali">Hasan ibn Ali</a> signed a <a href="/wiki/Hasan%E2%80%93Muawiya_treaty" title="Hasan–Muawiya treaty">peace treaty</a>, abdicating to <a href="/wiki/Muawiyah_I" title="Muawiyah I">Mu'awiyah</a>, beginning the <a href="/wiki/Umayyad_dynasty" class="mw-redirect" title="Umayyad dynasty">Umayyad dynasty</a>, in return that he not name his own successor.<sup id="cite_ref-196" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-196">[194]</a></sup> These disputes over religious and political leadership would give rise to schism in the Muslim community. The majority accepted the legitimacy of the first four leaders, and became known as Sunnis. A minority disagreed, and believed that only Ali and some of his descendants should rule; they became known as the Shia.<sup id="cite_ref-197" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-197">[195]</a></sup> Mu'awiyah appointed his son, <a href="/wiki/Yazid_I" title="Yazid I">Yazid I</a>, as successor and after Mu'awiyah's death in 680, the "<a href="/wiki/Second_Fitna" title="Second Fitna">Second Fitna</a>" broke out, where <a href="/wiki/Husayn_ibn_Ali" title="Husayn ibn Ali">Husayn ibn Ali</a> was killed at the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Karbala" title="Battle of Karbala">Battle of Karbala</a>, a significant event in Shia Islam.</p>
<p>The Umayyad dynasty conquered the <a href="/wiki/Maghreb" title="Maghreb">Maghreb</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Iberian_Peninsula" title="Iberian Peninsula">Iberian Peninsula</a>, <a href="/wiki/Gallia_Narbonensis" title="Gallia Narbonensis">Narbonnese Gaul</a> and <a href="/wiki/Sindh" title="Sindh">Sindh</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-198" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-198">[196]</a></sup> Local populations of Jews and indigenous Christians, persecuted as religious minorities and taxed heavily to finance the <a href="/wiki/Byzantine%E2%80%93Sassanid_Wars" class="mw-redirect" title="Byzantine–Sassanid Wars">Byzantine–Sassanid Wars</a>, often aided Muslims to take over their lands from the Byzantines and Persians, resulting in exceptionally speedy conquests.<sup id="cite_ref-199" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-199">[197]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-200" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-200">[198]</a></sup></p>
<p>The generation after the death of Muhammad but contemporaries of his companions are known as the <a href="/wiki/Tabi%27un" class="mw-redirect" title="Tabi'un">Tabi'un</a>, followed by the <a href="/w/index.php?title=Tabi%27_al-Tabi%27in&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Tabi' al-Tabi'in (page does not exist)">Tabi' al-Tabi'in</a>. The Caliph <a href="/wiki/Umar_ibn_Abd_al-Aziz" class="mw-redirect" title="Umar ibn Abd al-Aziz">Umar ibn Abd al-Aziz</a> set up the influential committee, "<a href="/wiki/The_Seven_Fuqaha_of_Medina" title="The Seven Fuqaha of Medina">The Seven Fuqaha of Medina</a>",<sup id="cite_ref-201" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-201">[199]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-202" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-202">[200]</a></sup> headed by <a href="/wiki/Qasim_ibn_Muhammad_ibn_Abu_Bakr" title="Qasim ibn Muhammad ibn Abu Bakr">Qasim ibn Muhammad ibn Abu Bakr</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-203" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-203">[201]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Malik_ibn_Anas" title="Malik ibn Anas">Malik ibn Anas</a> wrote one of the earliest books on Islamic jurisprudence, the <a href="/wiki/Muwatta_Imam_Malik" title="Muwatta Imam Malik">Muwatta</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-bewley.virtualave.net_204-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bewley.virtualave.net-204">[202]</a></sup> as a consensus of the opinion of those jurists.<sup id="cite_ref-Coulson_205-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Coulson-205">[203]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-206" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-206">[204]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-207" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-207">[205]</a></sup></p>
<p>The descendants of Muhammad's uncle <a href="/wiki/Abbas_ibn_Abd_al-Muttalib" class="mw-redirect" title="Abbas ibn Abd al-Muttalib">Abbas ibn Abd al-Muttalib</a> rallied discontented non-Arab converts (<i><a href="/wiki/Mawali" class="mw-redirect" title="Mawali">mawali</a></i>), poor Arabs, and some Shi'a against the Umayyads and overthrew them, inaugurating the <a href="/wiki/Abbasid" class="mw-redirect" title="Abbasid">Abbasid dynasty</a> in 750.<sup id="cite_ref-208" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-208">[206]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span id="Classical_era_(750–1258)"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Classical_era_.28750.E2.80.931258.29">Classical era (750–1258)</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Islamic_Golden_Age" title="Islamic Golden Age">Islamic Golden Age</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hadith_studies" title="Hadith studies">Hadith studies</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Islamic_philosophy" title="Islamic philosophy">Islamic philosophy</a></div>
<p>During this time, the <a href="/wiki/Delhi_Sultanate" title="Delhi Sultanate">Delhi Sultanate</a> took over northern parts of Indian subcontinent. Religious missions converted <a href="/wiki/Volga_Bulgaria" title="Volga Bulgaria">Volga Bulgaria</a> to Islam. Many Muslims also went to <a href="/wiki/Islam_in_China" title="Islam in China">China</a> to trade, virtually dominating the import and export industry of the <a href="/wiki/Song_dynasty" title="Song dynasty">Song dynasty</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-china_209-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-china-209">[207]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Cheshm_manuscript.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a6/Cheshm_manuscript.jpg/220px-Cheshm_manuscript.jpg" width="220" height="293" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a6/Cheshm_manuscript.jpg/330px-Cheshm_manuscript.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a6/Cheshm_manuscript.jpg/440px-Cheshm_manuscript.jpg 2x" data-file-width="450" data-file-height="600" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Cheshm_manuscript.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The eye, according to <a href="/wiki/Hunain_ibn_Ishaq" class="mw-redirect" title="Hunain ibn Ishaq">Hunain ibn Ishaq</a> from a manuscript dated circa 1200.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>This era is sometimes called the "<a href="/wiki/Islamic_Golden_Age" title="Islamic Golden Age">Islamic Golden Age</a>".<sup id="cite_ref-210" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-210">[208]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Public_hospital" title="Public hospital">Public hospitals</a> established during this time (called <a href="/wiki/Bimaristan" title="Bimaristan">Bimaristan</a> hospitals), are considered "the first hospitals" in the modern sense of the word,<sup id="cite_ref-211" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-211">[209]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-212" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-212">[210]</a></sup> and issued the first medical diplomas to license <a href="/wiki/Doctor_of_Medicine" title="Doctor of Medicine">doctors</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Alatas_213-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Alatas-213">[211]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-214" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-214">[212]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Guinness_World_Records" title="Guinness World Records">Guinness World Records</a> recognizes the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Al_Karaouine" class="mw-redirect" title="University of Al Karaouine">University of Al Karaouine</a>, founded in 859, as the world's oldest degree-granting university.<sup id="cite_ref-215" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-215">[213]</a></sup> The doctorate is argued to date back to the <a href="/wiki/Ijazah" title="Ijazah">licenses to teach</a> in Muslim <a href="/wiki/Law_school" title="Law school">law schools</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Makdisi_216-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Makdisi-216">[214]</a></sup> Standards of experimental and <a href="/wiki/Quantification_(science)" title="Quantification (science)">quantification</a> techniques, as well as the tradition of citation,<sup id="cite_ref-Ahmed_217-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ahmed-217">[215]</a></sup> were introduced. An important pioneer in this, <a href="/wiki/Ibn_al-Haytham" title="Ibn al-Haytham">Ibn al-Haytham</a> is regarded as the father of the modern <a href="/wiki/Scientific_method" title="Scientific method">scientific method</a> and often referred to as the "world's first true scientist".<sup id="cite_ref-Haq_218-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Haq-218">[216]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-219" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-219">[217]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-220" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-220">[218]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-221" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-221">[219]</a></sup> The government paid scientists the equivalent salary of professional athletes today.<sup id="cite_ref-Ahmed_217-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ahmed-217">[215]</a></sup> It is argued that the data used by <a href="/wiki/Copernicus" class="mw-redirect" title="Copernicus">Copernicus</a> for his <a href="/wiki/Heliocentric" class="mw-redirect" title="Heliocentric">heliocentric</a> conclusions was gathered and that <a href="/wiki/Al-Jahiz" title="Al-Jahiz">Al-Jahiz</a> proposed a theory of <a href="/wiki/Natural_selection" title="Natural selection">natural selection</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-222" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-222">[220]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-223" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-223">[221]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Rumi" title="Rumi">Rumi</a> wrote some of the finest Persian poetry and is still one of the best selling poets in America.<sup id="cite_ref-224" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-224">[222]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-225" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-225">[223]</a></sup> Legal institutions introduced include the <a href="/wiki/Trust_law" title="Trust law">trust</a> and <a href="/wiki/Charitable_trust" title="Charitable trust">charitable trust</a> (<a href="/wiki/Waqf" title="Waqf">Waqf</a>).<sup id="cite_ref-226" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-226">[224]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-227" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-227">[225]</a></sup></p>
<p>Al-Shafi'i codified a method to determine the reliability of hadith.<sup id="cite_ref-228" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-228">[226]</a></sup> During the early Abbasid era, the major <a href="/wiki/Six_major_Hadith_collections" class="mw-redirect" title="Six major Hadith collections">Sunni hadith collections</a> were compiled by scholars such as <a href="/wiki/Muhammad_al-Bukhari" title="Muhammad al-Bukhari">Bukhari</a> and <a href="/wiki/Muslim_ibn_al-Hajjaj" title="Muslim ibn al-Hajjaj">Muslim</a> while major <a href="/wiki/The_Four_Books" title="The Four Books">Shia hadith collections</a> by scholars such as <a href="/wiki/Muhammad_ibn_Ya%27qub_al-Kulayni" title="Muhammad ibn Ya'qub al-Kulayni">Al-Kulayni</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ibn_Babawayh" title="Ibn Babawayh">Ibn Babawayh</a> were also compiled. The <a href="/wiki/Ja%27fari_jurisprudence" title="Ja'fari jurisprudence">Ja'fari jurisprudence</a> was formed from the teachings of <a href="/wiki/Ja%27far_al-Sadiq" title="Ja'far al-Sadiq">Ja'far al-Sadiq</a> while the four Sunni <a href="/wiki/Madh%27hab" class="mw-redirect" title="Madh'hab">Madh'habs</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Hanafi" title="Hanafi">Hanafi</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hanbali" title="Hanbali">Hanbali</a>, <a href="/wiki/Maliki" title="Maliki">Maliki</a> and <a href="/wiki/Shafi%27i" title="Shafi'i">Shafi'i</a>, were established around the teachings of <a href="/wiki/Ab%C5%AB_%E1%B8%A4an%C4%ABfa" class="mw-redirect" title="Abū Ḥanīfa">Abū Ḥanīfa</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ahmad_bin_Hanbal" class="mw-redirect" title="Ahmad bin Hanbal">Ahmad bin Hanbal</a>, <a href="/wiki/Malik_ibn_Anas" title="Malik ibn Anas">Malik ibn Anas</a> and <a href="/wiki/Al-Shafi%27i" class="mw-redirect" title="Al-Shafi'i">al-Shafi'i</a> respectively. In the 9th century, al-Shafi'i provided a theoretical basis for Islamic law by codifying the principles of jurisprudence in his book <i>ar-Risālah</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-229" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-229">[227]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Al-Tabari" title="Al-Tabari">Al-Tabari</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ibn_Kathir" title="Ibn Kathir">Ibn Kathir</a> completed the most commonly cited commentaries on the Quran, the <a href="/wiki/Tafsir_al-Tabari" title="Tafsir al-Tabari">Tafsir al-Tabari</a> in the 9th century and the <a href="/wiki/Tafsir_ibn_Kathir" class="mw-redirect" title="Tafsir ibn Kathir">Tafsir ibn Kathir</a> in the 14th century, respectively. Philosophers <a href="/wiki/Al-Farabi" title="Al-Farabi">Al-Farabi</a> and <a href="/wiki/Avicenna" title="Avicenna">Avicenna</a> sought to incorporate Greek principles into Islamic theology, while others like <a href="/wiki/Al-Ghazali" title="Al-Ghazali">Al-Ghazali</a> argued against them and ultimately prevailed.<sup id="cite_ref-230" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-230">[228]</a></sup></p>
<p>Caliphs such as <a href="/wiki/Mamun_al_Rashid" class="mw-redirect" title="Mamun al Rashid">Mamun al Rashid</a> and <a href="/wiki/Al-Mu%27tasim" title="Al-Mu'tasim">Al-Mu'tasim</a> made the <a href="/wiki/Mutazilite" class="mw-redirect" title="Mutazilite">mutazilite</a> philosophy an official creed and imposed it upon Muslims to follow. Mu'tazila was a Greek influenced school of speculative theology called <a href="/wiki/Kalam" title="Kalam">kalam</a>, which refers to <a href="/wiki/Dialectic" title="Dialectic">dialectic</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-231" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-231">[229]</a></sup> Many orthodox Muslims rejected <a href="/wiki/Mutazilite" class="mw-redirect" title="Mutazilite">mutazilite</a> doctrines and condemned their idea of the creation of the Quran. In inquisitions, Imam Hanbal refused to conform and was tortured and sent to an unlit <a href="/wiki/Baghdad" title="Baghdad">Baghdad</a> prison cell for nearly thirty months.<sup id="cite_ref-232" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-232">[230]</a></sup> The other branch of kalam was the <a href="/wiki/Ash%27ari" title="Ash'ari">Ash'ari</a> school founded by <a href="/wiki/Al-Ash%27ari" class="mw-redirect" title="Al-Ash'ari">Al-Ash'ari</a>.</p>
<p>Some Muslims began to question the piety of indulgence in a worldly life and emphasized poverty, humility and avoidance of <a href="/wiki/Islamic_views_on_sin" title="Islamic views on sin">sin</a> based on renunciation of bodily desires. Ascetics such as <a href="/wiki/Hasan_al-Basri" class="mw-redirect" title="Hasan al-Basri">Hasan al-Basri</a> would inspire a movement that would evolve into Tasawwuf (Sufism).<sup id="cite_ref-233" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-233">[231]</a></sup> Beginning in the 13th century, Sufism underwent a transformation, largely because of efforts to legitimize and reorganize the movement by <a href="/wiki/Al-Ghazali" title="Al-Ghazali">Al-Ghazali</a>, who developed the model of the <a href="/wiki/Tariqa" title="Tariqa">Sufi order</a>—a community of spiritual teachers and students.<sup id="cite_ref-234" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-234">[232]</a></sup></p>
<p>The first Muslims states independent of a unified Muslim state emerged from the Berber Revolt (739/740-743). In 930, the Ismaili group known as the <a href="/wiki/Qarmatians" title="Qarmatians">Qarmatians</a> unsuccessfully rebelled against the Abbassids, sacked Mecca and stole the Black Stone, which was eventually retrieved.<sup id="cite_ref-235" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-235">[233]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Mongol_Empire" title="Mongol Empire">Mongol Empire</a> put an end to the Abbassid dynasty in 1258.<sup id="cite_ref-236" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-236">[234]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span id="Pre-Modern_era_(1258–20th_century)"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Pre-Modern_era_.281258.E2.80.9320th_century.29">Pre-Modern era (1258–20th century)</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Portrait_Caliph_Abdulmecid_II.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8e/Portrait_Caliph_Abdulmecid_II.jpg/220px-Portrait_Caliph_Abdulmecid_II.jpg" width="220" height="297" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8e/Portrait_Caliph_Abdulmecid_II.jpg/330px-Portrait_Caliph_Abdulmecid_II.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8e/Portrait_Caliph_Abdulmecid_II.jpg/440px-Portrait_Caliph_Abdulmecid_II.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1185" data-file-height="1600" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Portrait_Caliph_Abdulmecid_II.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Abd%C3%BClmecid_II" class="mw-redirect" title="Abdülmecid II">Abdülmecid II</a> was the last Caliph of Islam from the <a href="/wiki/Ottoman_dynasty" title="Ottoman dynasty">Ottoman dynasty</a>.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Islam spread with Muslim trade networks and Sufi orders activity that extended into <a href="/wiki/Sub-Saharan_Africa" title="Sub-Saharan Africa">Sub-Saharan Africa</a>, Central Asia and the <a href="/wiki/Malay_archipelago" class="mw-redirect" title="Malay archipelago">Malay archipelago</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-EoI-Islam_237-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-EoI-Islam-237">[235]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-238" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-238">[236]</a></sup> Under the <a href="/wiki/Ottoman_Empire" title="Ottoman Empire">Ottoman Empire</a>, Islam spread to Southeast Europe.<sup id="cite_ref-239" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-239">[237]</a></sup> The Muslims in China who were descended from earlier immigration began to assimilate by adopting Chinese names and <a href="/wiki/Chinese_culture" title="Chinese culture">culture</a> while <a href="/wiki/Nanjing" title="Nanjing">Nanjing</a> became an important center of Islamic study.<sup id="cite_ref-240" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-240">[238]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-241" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-241">[239]</a></sup></p>
<p>The Muslim world was generally in political decline starting the 1800s, especially relative to the non-Muslim European powers. This decline was evident culturally; while <a href="/wiki/Taqi_al-Din_Muhammad_ibn_Ma%27ruf" class="mw-redirect" title="Taqi al-Din Muhammad ibn Ma'ruf">Taqi al-Din</a> founded an observatory in <a href="/wiki/Istanbul" title="Istanbul">Istanbul</a> and the Jai Singh Observatory was built in the 18th century, there was not a single Muslim country with a major observatory by the twentieth century.<sup id="cite_ref-242" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-242">[240]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Reconquista" title="Reconquista">Reconquista</a>, launched against Muslim <a href="/wiki/Taifa" title="Taifa">principalities</a> in <a href="/wiki/Iberian_Peninsula" title="Iberian Peninsula">Iberia</a>, succeeded in 1492. By the 19th century the <a href="/wiki/British_Raj" title="British Raj">British Empire</a> had formally ended the Mughal dynasty in India.<sup id="cite_ref-Lapidus_2002.2C_pp.358.2C378.E2.80.93380.2C624_243-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Lapidus_2002.2C_pp.358.2C378.E2.80.93380.2C624-243">[241]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Fall_of_the_Ottoman_Empire" class="mw-redirect" title="Fall of the Ottoman Empire">Ottoman Empire disintegrated</a> after <a href="/wiki/World_War_I" title="World War I">World War I</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Caliphate" title="Caliphate">Caliphate</a> was abolished in 1924.<sup id="cite_ref-244" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-244">[242]</a></sup></p>
<p>The majority and oldest group among Shia at that time, the <a href="/wiki/Zaydis" class="mw-redirect" title="Zaydis">Zaydis</a>, named after the great grandson of Ali, the scholar <a href="/wiki/Zayd_ibn_Ali" title="Zayd ibn Ali">Zayd ibn Ali</a>, used the Hanafi jurisprudence, as did most Sunnis.<sup id="cite_ref-books.google.co.uk-mae_245-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-books.google.co.uk-mae-245">[243]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Arab-Israeli_Conflict_Page_917_246-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Arab-Israeli_Conflict_Page_917-246">[244]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-The_Iraq_Effect_Page_91_247-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-The_Iraq_Effect_Page_91-247">[245]</a></sup> The Shia <a href="/wiki/Safavid_dynasty" title="Safavid dynasty">Safavid dynasty</a> rose to power in 1501 and later conquered all of Iran.<sup id="cite_ref-248" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-248">[246]</a></sup> The ensuing mandatory <a href="/wiki/Safavid_conversion_of_Iran_to_Shia_Islam" title="Safavid conversion of Iran to Shia Islam">conversion of Iran to Twelver Shia Islam</a> for the largely Sunni population also ensured the final dominance of the Twelver sect within Shiism over the <a href="/wiki/Zaidiyyah" title="Zaidiyyah">Zaidi</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ismaili" class="mw-redirect" title="Ismaili">Ismaili</a> sects.<sup id="cite_ref-249" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-249">[247]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Nader_Shah" title="Nader Shah">Nader Shah</a>, who overthrew the Safavids, attempted to improve relations with Sunnis by propagating the integration of Shiism by calling it the Jaafari Madh'hab.<sup id="cite_ref-250" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-250">[248]</a></sup></p>
<p>A revival movement during this period was an 18th-century <a href="/wiki/Salafi" class="mw-redirect" title="Salafi">Salafi</a> movement led by <a href="/wiki/Muhammad_ibn_Abdul_Wahhab" class="mw-redirect" title="Muhammad ibn Abdul Wahhab">Ibn Abd al-Wahhab</a> in today's Saudi Arabia. Referred to as <a href="/wiki/Wahhabi" class="mw-redirect" title="Wahhabi">Wahhabi</a>, their self designation is Muwahiddun (unitarians). Building upon earlier efforts such as those by <a href="/wiki/Ibn_Taymiyyah" title="Ibn Taymiyyah">Ibn Taymiyyah</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ibn_al-Qayyim" class="mw-redirect" title="Ibn al-Qayyim">Ibn al-Qayyim</a>, the movement allegedly seeks to uphold monotheism and purify Islam of what they see as later <a href="/wiki/Bidah" class="mw-redirect" title="Bidah">innovations</a>. Their zeal against <a href="/wiki/Idolatry_in_Islam" class="mw-redirect" title="Idolatry in Islam">idolatrous</a> shrines led to the desecration of shrines around the world, including that of <a href="/wiki/Destruction_of_early_Islamic_heritage_sites_in_Saudi_Arabia" title="Destruction of early Islamic heritage sites in Saudi Arabia">Muhammad and his companions in Mecca and Medina</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-251" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-251">[249]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-252" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-252">[250]</a></sup> In the 19th century, the <a href="/wiki/Deobandi" title="Deobandi">Deobandi</a> and <a href="/wiki/Barelwi" class="mw-redirect" title="Barelwi">Barelwi</a> movements were initiated.</p>
<h3><span id="Modern_times_(20th_century–present)"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Modern_times_.2820th_century.E2.80.93present.29">Modern times (20th century–present)</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Islamic_revival" title="Islamic revival">Islamic revival</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Flag_of_OIC.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/05/Flag_of_OIC.svg/220px-Flag_of_OIC.svg.png" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/05/Flag_of_OIC.svg/330px-Flag_of_OIC.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/05/Flag_of_OIC.svg/440px-Flag_of_OIC.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="1200" data-file-height="800" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Flag_of_OIC.svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The flag of the <a href="/wiki/Organisation_of_Islamic_Cooperation" title="Organisation of Islamic Cooperation">Organisation of Islamic Cooperation</a>.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Contact with industrialized nations brought Muslim populations to new areas through economic migration. Many Muslims migrated as indentured servants, from mostly India and <a href="/wiki/Indonesia" title="Indonesia">Indonesia</a>, to the <a href="/wiki/Caribbean" title="Caribbean">Caribbean</a>, forming the largest Muslim populations by percentage in the Americas.<sup id="cite_ref-253" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-253">[251]</a></sup> The resulting urbanization and increase in trade in <a href="/wiki/Sub-Saharan_Africa" title="Sub-Saharan Africa">sub-Saharan Africa</a> brought Muslims to settle in new areas and spread their faith, likely doubling its Muslim population between 1869 and 1914.<sup id="cite_ref-254" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-254">[252]</a></sup> Muslim immigrants began arriving, many as <a href="/wiki/Guest_workers" class="mw-redirect" title="Guest workers">guest workers</a> and largely from former colonies, in several Western European nations since the 1960s.</p>
<p>There are more and more new Muslim intellectuals who increasingly separate perennial Islamic beliefs from archaic cultural traditions.<sup id="cite_ref-255" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-255">[253]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Liberal_Islam" class="mw-redirect" title="Liberal Islam">Liberal Islam</a> is a movement that attempts to reconcile religious tradition with modern norms of secular governance and human rights. Its supporters say that there are multiple ways to read Islam's sacred texts, and they stress the need to leave room for "independent thought on religious matters".<sup id="cite_ref-256" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-256">[254]</a></sup> Women's issues receive significant weight in the modern discourse on Islam.<sup id="cite_ref-Rippin_2001.2C_p.288_257-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Rippin_2001.2C_p.288-257">[255]</a></sup></p>
<p>Secular powers such as the Chinese <a href="/wiki/Red_Guards_(China)" class="mw-redirect" title="Red Guards (China)">Red Guards</a> closed many mosques and destroyed Qurans,<sup id="cite_ref-258" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-258">[256]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/People%27s_Socialist_Republic_of_Albania" title="People's Socialist Republic of Albania">Communist Albania</a> became the first country to ban the practice of every religion.<sup id="cite_ref-259" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-259">[257]</a></sup> About half a million Muslims were killed in <a href="/wiki/Khmer_Rouge" title="Khmer Rouge">Cambodia</a> by communists who, it is argued, viewed them as their primary enemy and wished to exterminate them since they stood out and worshipped their own god.<sup id="cite_ref-260" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-260">[258]</a></sup> In <a href="/wiki/Turkey" title="Turkey">Turkey</a>, the military carried out coups to oust Islamist governments, and headscarves were banned in official buildings, as also happened in <a href="/wiki/Tunisia" title="Tunisia">Tunisia</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-261" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-261">[259]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-262" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-262">[260]</a></sup></p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Jamal-al-Din_al-Afghani" class="mw-redirect" title="Jamal-al-Din al-Afghani">Jamal-al-Din al-Afghani</a>, along with his acolyte <a href="/wiki/Muhammad_Abduh" title="Muhammad Abduh">Muhammad Abduh</a>, have been credited as forerunners of the <a href="/wiki/Islamic_revival" title="Islamic revival">Islamic revival</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-263" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-263">[261]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Abul_A%27la_Maududi" title="Abul A'la Maududi">Abul A'la Maududi</a> helped influence modern <a href="/wiki/Political_Islam" title="Political Islam">political Islam</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-264" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-264">[262]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Islamist" class="mw-redirect" title="Islamist">Islamist</a> groups such as the <a href="/wiki/Muslim_Brotherhood" title="Muslim Brotherhood">Muslim Brotherhood</a> advocate Islam as a comprehensive political solution, often in spite of being banned.<sup id="cite_ref-265" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-265">[263]</a></sup> In <a href="/wiki/Iran" title="Iran">Iran</a>, <a href="/wiki/Iranian_Revolution" title="Iranian Revolution">revolution</a> replaced a <a href="/wiki/Secularism" title="Secularism">secular</a> regime with an <a href="/wiki/Islamic_state" title="Islamic state">Islamic state</a>. In <a href="/wiki/Turkey" title="Turkey">Turkey</a>, the Islamist <a href="/wiki/Justice_and_Development_Party_(Turkey)" title="Justice and Development Party (Turkey)">AK Party</a> has democratically been in power for about a decade, while Islamist parties did well in elections following the <a href="/wiki/Arab_Spring" title="Arab Spring">Arab Spring</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-266" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-266">[264]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Organisation_of_Islamic_Cooperation" title="Organisation of Islamic Cooperation">Organisation of Islamic Cooperation</a> (OIC), consisting of <a href="/wiki/Islam_by_country" title="Islam by country">Muslim countries</a>, was established in 1969 after the burning of the <a href="/wiki/Al-Aqsa_Mosque" title="Al-Aqsa Mosque">Al-Aqsa Mosque</a> in <a href="/wiki/Jerusalem" title="Jerusalem">Jerusalem</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-267" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-267">[265]</a></sup></p>
<p>Piety appears to be deepening worldwide.<sup id="cite_ref-268" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-268">[266]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-economist_269-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-economist-269">[267]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-270" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-270">[268]</a></sup> In many places, the prevalence of the <a href="/wiki/Hijab" title="Hijab">hijab</a> is growing increasingly common<sup id="cite_ref-271" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-271">[269]</a></sup> and the percentage of Muslims favoring Sharia laws has increased.<sup id="cite_ref-272" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-272">[270]</a></sup> With religious guidance increasingly available electronically, Muslims are able to access views that are strict enough for them rather than rely on state clerics who are often seen as stooges.<sup id="cite_ref-economist_269-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-economist-269">[267]</a></sup></p>
<p>It is estimated that, by 2050, the number of Muslims will nearly equal the number of Christians around the world, "driven primarily by differences in fertility rates and the size of youth populations among the world's major religions, as well as by people switching faiths."<sup id="cite_ref-pew2015_273-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-pew2015-273">[271]</a></sup> Perhaps as a sign of these changes, most experts agree that Islam is growing faster than any other faith in <a href="/wiki/East_Africa" title="East Africa">East</a> and West Africa.<sup id="cite_ref-274" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-274">[272]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-275" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-275">[273]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Denominations">Denominations</span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Islamic_schools_and_branches" title="Islamic schools and branches">Islamic schools and branches</a></div>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Shia%E2%80%93Sunni_relations" title="Shia–Sunni relations">Shia–Sunni relations</a></div>
<div class="center">
<div class="thumb tnone">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:752px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Islam_branches_and_schools.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/66/Islam_branches_and_schools.svg/750px-Islam_branches_and_schools.svg.png" width="750" height="488" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/66/Islam_branches_and_schools.svg/1125px-Islam_branches_and_schools.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/66/Islam_branches_and_schools.svg/1500px-Islam_branches_and_schools.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="1032" data-file-height="672" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Islam_branches_and_schools.svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
An overview of the major schools and branches of Islam.</div>
</div>
</div>
</div>

<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Sunni_Islam" title="Sunni Islam">Sunni Islam</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Sahih_Al-Bukhari_in_English.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/Sahih_Al-Bukhari_in_English.png/220px-Sahih_Al-Bukhari_in_English.png" width="220" height="202" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/Sahih_Al-Bukhari_in_English.png/330px-Sahih_Al-Bukhari_in_English.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/Sahih_Al-Bukhari_in_English.png/440px-Sahih_Al-Bukhari_in_English.png 2x" data-file-width="908" data-file-height="835" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Sahih_Al-Bukhari_in_English.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Sahih_Al-Bukhari" class="mw-redirect" title="Sahih Al-Bukhari">Sahih Al-Bukhari</a>, one of the <a href="/wiki/Al-Kutub_Al-Sittah" class="mw-redirect" title="Al-Kutub Al-Sittah">six Sunni hadith books</a>.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The largest denomination in Islam is Sunni Islam, which makes up 75%–90% of all Muslims<sup id="cite_ref-Sunni-eb_32-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Sunni-eb-32">[31]</a></sup> and is arguably the world's largest religious denomination.<sup id="cite_ref-276" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-276">[274]</a></sup> Sunni Muslims also go by the name <i>Ahl as-Sunnah</i> which means "people of the tradition [of Muhammad]".<sup id="cite_ref-Lippman_7-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Lippman-7">[6]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Britannica_277-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Britannica-277">[275]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-278" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-278">[276]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-279" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-279">[277]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-280" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-280">[278]</a></sup></p>
<p>Sunnis believe that the first four <a href="/wiki/Caliph" class="mw-redirect" title="Caliph">caliphs</a> were the rightful successors to Muhammad; since God did not specify any particular leaders to succeed him and those leaders were elected. Sunnis believe that anyone who is righteous and just could be a caliph but they have to act according to the Quran and the Hadith, the example of Muhammad and give the people their rights.</p>
<p>The Sunnis follow the Quran and the Hadith, which are recorded in sunni traditions known as <a href="/wiki/Al-Kutub_Al-Sittah" class="mw-redirect" title="Al-Kutub Al-Sittah">Al-Kutub Al-Sittah</a> (six major books). For legal matters derived from the Quran or the Hadith, many follow four sunni <a href="/wiki/Madhhab" title="Madhhab">madh'habs</a> (schools of thought): <a href="/wiki/Hanafi" title="Hanafi">Hanafi</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hanbali" title="Hanbali">Hanbali</a>, <a href="/wiki/Maliki" title="Maliki">Maliki</a> and <a href="/wiki/Shafi%27i" title="Shafi'i">Shafi'i</a>. All four accept the validity of the others and a Muslim may choose any one that he or she finds agreeable.<sup id="cite_ref-281" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-281">[279]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Ahl_al-Hadith" title="Ahl al-Hadith">Ahl al-Hadith</a> is a movement that deemphasized sources of jurisprudence outside the quran and sunnah, such as informed opinion (ra'y).</p>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Salafi_movement" title="Salafi movement">Salafi movement</a> claim to take the first three generations of Muslims, known as the <a href="/wiki/Salaf" title="Salaf">salaf</a>, as exemplary models.<sup id="cite_ref-282" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-282">[280]</a></sup> In the 18th century, <a href="/wiki/Muhammad_ibn_Abd_al-Wahhab" title="Muhammad ibn Abd al-Wahhab">Muhammad ibn Abd al-Wahhab</a> led a salafi movement, referred by outsiders as <a href="/wiki/Wahhabism" title="Wahhabism">Wahhabism</a>, in modern-day <a href="/wiki/Saudi_Arabia" title="Saudi Arabia">Saudi Arabia</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Deobandi_movement" class="mw-redirect" title="Deobandi movement">Deobandi movement</a> is a reformist movement originating in South Asia, influenced by the Wahhabi movement.<sup id="cite_ref-283" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-283">[281]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Shia">Shia</span></h3>

<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Shia_Islam" title="Shia Islam">Shia Islam</a></div>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Safavid_conversion_of_Iran_to_Shia_Islam" title="Safavid conversion of Iran to Shia Islam">Safavid conversion of Iran to Shia Islam</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Kerbela_Hussein_Moschee.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4f/Kerbela_Hussein_Moschee.jpg/220px-Kerbela_Hussein_Moschee.jpg" width="220" height="146" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4f/Kerbela_Hussein_Moschee.jpg/330px-Kerbela_Hussein_Moschee.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4f/Kerbela_Hussein_Moschee.jpg/440px-Kerbela_Hussein_Moschee.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2464" data-file-height="1632" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Kerbela_Hussein_Moschee.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The <a href="/wiki/Imam_Husayn_Shrine" title="Imam Husayn Shrine">Imam Hussein Shrine</a> in <a href="/wiki/Karbala" title="Karbala">Karbala</a>, Iraq is a holy site for Shia Muslims.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The Shia constitute 10–20% of Islam and are its second-largest branch.<sup id="cite_ref-Shia_33-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Shia-33">[32]</a></sup></p>
<p>While the Sunnis believe that a Caliph should be elected by the community, Shia's believe that Muhammad appointed his son-in-law, <a href="/wiki/Ali_ibn_Abi_Talib" class="mw-redirect" title="Ali ibn Abi Talib">Ali ibn Abi Talib</a>, as his successor and only certain descendants of Ali could be Imams. As a result, they believe that <a href="/wiki/Ali_ibn_Abi_Talib" class="mw-redirect" title="Ali ibn Abi Talib">Ali ibn Abi Talib</a> was the first <i>Imam</i> (leader), rejecting the legitimacy of the previous Muslim caliphs <a href="/wiki/Abu_Bakr" title="Abu Bakr">Abu Bakr</a>, <a href="/wiki/Uthman_ibn_al-Affan" class="mw-redirect" title="Uthman ibn al-Affan">Uthman ibn al-Affan</a> and <a href="/wiki/Umar_ibn_al-Khattab" class="mw-redirect" title="Umar ibn al-Khattab">Umar ibn al-Khattab</a>. Other points of contention include certain practices viewed as <a href="/wiki/Bid%E2%80%98ah" title="Bid‘ah">innovating</a> the religion, such as the mourning practice of <a href="/wiki/Tatbir" title="Tatbir">tatbir</a>, and the cursing of figures revered by Sunnis. However, <a href="/wiki/Jafar_al-Sadiq" class="mw-redirect" title="Jafar al-Sadiq">Jafar al-Sadiq</a> himself disapproved of people who disapproved of his great grand father <a href="/wiki/Abu_Bakr" title="Abu Bakr">Abu Bakr</a> and <a href="/wiki/Zayd_ibn_Ali" title="Zayd ibn Ali">Zayd ibn Ali</a> revered Abu Bakr and <a href="/wiki/Umar" title="Umar">Umar</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Tabar.C4.AB.2C_Carole_Hillenbrand_1989.2C_p37_284-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Tabar.C4.AB.2C_Carole_Hillenbrand_1989.2C_p37-284">[282]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Religion_Vol_1987.2C_p243_285-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Religion_Vol_1987.2C_p243-285">[283]</a></sup> More recently, <a href="/wiki/Ali_Khamenei" title="Ali Khamenei">Ali Khamenei</a><sup id="cite_ref-286" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-286">[284]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Grand_Ayatollah" class="mw-redirect" title="Grand Ayatollah">Grand Ayatollah</a> <a href="/wiki/Ali_al-Sistani" title="Ali al-Sistani">Ali al-Sistani</a><sup id="cite_ref-287" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-287">[285]</a></sup> condemned the practice.</p>
<p>Shia Islam has several branches, the most prominent being the <a href="/wiki/Twelvers" class="mw-redirect" title="Twelvers">Twelvers</a> (the largest branch), <a href="/wiki/Zaidiyyah" title="Zaidiyyah">Zaidis</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ismaili" class="mw-redirect" title="Ismaili">Ismailis</a>. Different branches accept different descendants of Ali as Imams. After the death of Imam <a href="/wiki/Jafar_al-Sadiq" class="mw-redirect" title="Jafar al-Sadiq">Jafar al-Sadiq</a> who is considered the sixth Imam by the <a href="/wiki/Twelvers" class="mw-redirect" title="Twelvers">Twelvers</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Ismaili" class="mw-redirect" title="Ismaili">Ismaili</a>'s, the Ismailis recognized his son Isma'il ibn Jafar as his successor whereas the Twelver Shia's (Ithna Asheri) followed his other son <a href="/wiki/Musa_al-Kadhim" title="Musa al-Kadhim">Musa al-Kadhim</a> as the seventh Imam. The <a href="/wiki/Zaidiyyah" title="Zaidiyyah">Zaydis</a> consider <a href="/wiki/Zayd_ibn_Ali" title="Zayd ibn Ali">Zayd ibn Ali</a>, the uncle of Imam <a href="/wiki/Jafar_al-Sadiq" class="mw-redirect" title="Jafar al-Sadiq">Jafar al-Sadiq</a>, as their fifth Imam, and follow a different line of succession after him. Other smaller groups include the <a href="/wiki/Mustaali" class="mw-redirect" title="Mustaali">Bohra</a> as well as the <a href="/wiki/Alawites" title="Alawites">Alawites</a> and <a href="/wiki/Alevi" class="mw-redirect" title="Alevi">Alevi</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-288" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-288">[286]</a></sup> Some Shia branches label other Shia branches that do not agree with their doctrine as <a href="/wiki/Ghulat" title="Ghulat">Ghulat</a>.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Sufism">Sufism</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Sufism" title="Sufism">Sufism</a></div>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Sufi%E2%80%93Salafi_relations" title="Sufi–Salafi relations">Sufi–Salafi relations</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Mevlana_Konya.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f7/Mevlana_Konya.jpg/220px-Mevlana_Konya.jpg" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f7/Mevlana_Konya.jpg/330px-Mevlana_Konya.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f7/Mevlana_Konya.jpg/440px-Mevlana_Konya.jpg 2x" data-file-width="500" data-file-height="375" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Mevlana_Konya.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Mawlānā <a href="/wiki/Rumi" title="Rumi">Rumi</a>'s tomb, <a href="/wiki/Konya" title="Konya">Konya</a>, Turkey</div>
</div>
</div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Sufism" title="Sufism">Sufism</a>, or tasawwuf (<a href="/wiki/Arabic_language" class="mw-redirect" title="Arabic language">Arabic</a>: <span lang="ar" dir="rtl" xml:lang="ar">تصوف</span>‎‎), is a <a href="/wiki/Mystical" class="mw-redirect" title="Mystical">mystical</a>-<a href="/wiki/Ascetic" class="mw-redirect" title="Ascetic">ascetic</a> approach to Islam that seeks to find a direct <a href="/wiki/Divine_presence" title="Divine presence">personal experience of God</a>. It is not a sect of Islam and its adherents belong to the various Muslim denominations. Classical Sufi scholars have focused on the reparation of the heart and turning it away from all else but God by making use of "intuitive and emotional faculties" that one must be trained to use.<sup id="cite_ref-289" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-289">[287]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-290" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-290">[288]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-291" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-291">[289]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Hasan_al-Basri" class="mw-redirect" title="Hasan al-Basri">Hasan al-Basri</a> was inspired by the ideas of piety and condemnation of worldliness preached by Muhammad and these ideas were later further developed by <a href="/wiki/Al-Ghazali" title="Al-Ghazali">Al-Ghazali</a>. Traditional Sufis, such as <a href="/wiki/Bayazid_Bastami" title="Bayazid Bastami">Bayazid Bastami</a>, <a href="/wiki/Jalaluddin_Rumi" class="mw-redirect" title="Jalaluddin Rumi">Jalaluddin Rumi</a>, <a href="/wiki/Haji_Bektash_Veli" title="Haji Bektash Veli">Haji Bektash Veli</a>, <a href="/wiki/Junaid_Baghdadi" class="mw-redirect" title="Junaid Baghdadi">Junaid Baghdadi</a>, and Al-Ghazali, argued for Sufism being based upon the tenets of Islam and the teachings of <a href="/wiki/Muhammad" title="Muhammad">Muhammad</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-292" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-292">[290]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-293" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-293">[291]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-chittick_294-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-chittick-294">[292]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-nasr_295-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-nasr-295">[293]</a></sup> Sufi practices such as veneration of saints have faced stiff opposition from followers of <a href="/wiki/Salafism" class="mw-redirect" title="Salafism">Salafism</a> and <a href="/wiki/Wahhabism" title="Wahhabism">Wahhabism</a>, who have sometimes physically attacked Sufi places of worship, leading to deterioration in <a href="/wiki/Sufi%E2%80%93Salafi_relations" title="Sufi–Salafi relations">Sufi–Salafi relations</a>.</p>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Barelvi" title="Barelvi">Barelvi</a> movement is a Sufi-influenced revivalist movement within <a href="/wiki/Sunni_Islam" title="Sunni Islam">Sunni Islam</a> with over 200 million followers,<sup id="cite_ref-296" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-296">[294]</a></sup> largely in South Asia.<sup id="cite_ref-journals.cambridge.org_297-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-journals.cambridge.org-297">[295]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-298" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-298">[296]</a></sup> Sufism enjoyed a strong revival in central Asia and South Asia. Central Asia is considered to be a center of Sufism. Sufism has played a significant role in fighting against Tsars of Russia and Soviet colonization. Here, Sufis and their different orders are the main religious sources.<sup id="cite_ref-299" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-299">[297]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-300" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-300">[298]</a></sup> Sufism is also strong in African countries such as <a href="/wiki/Tunisia" title="Tunisia">Tunisia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Algeria" title="Algeria">Algeria</a>, <a href="/wiki/Morocco" title="Morocco">Morocco</a>, <a href="/wiki/Senegal" title="Senegal">Senegal</a>, <a href="/wiki/Chad" title="Chad">Chad</a> and <a href="/wiki/Niger" title="Niger">Niger</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Pew_301-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Pew-301">[299]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-302" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-302">[300]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Other_denominations">Other denominations</span></h3>
<ul>
<li><a href="/wiki/Ahmadiyya" title="Ahmadiyya">Ahmadiyya</a> is an Islamic reform movement (with Sunni roots) founded by <a href="/wiki/Mirza_Ghulam_Ahmad" title="Mirza Ghulam Ahmad">Mirza Ghulam Ahmad</a><sup id="cite_ref-303" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-303">[301]</a></sup> that began in <a href="/wiki/India" title="India">India</a> in 1889 and is practiced by 10 to 20 million<sup id="cite_ref-ahmadi_304-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ahmadi-304">[302]</a></sup> Muslims around the world. Ahmad claimed to have fulfilled the prophecies concerning the arrival of the 'Imam Mahdi' and the 'Promised Messiah'.</li>
<li>The <a href="/wiki/Ibadi" title="Ibadi">Ibadi</a> is a sect that dates back to the early days of Islam and is a branch of <a href="/wiki/Kharijite" class="mw-redirect" title="Kharijite">Kharijite</a> and is practiced by 1.45&#160;million Muslims around the world.<sup id="cite_ref-305" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-305">[303]</a></sup> Unlike most Kharijite groups, Ibadism does not regard sinful Muslims as unbelievers.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Mahdavia" title="Mahdavia">Mahdavia</a> is an Islamic sect that believes in a 15th-century Mahdi, Muhammad Jaunpuri</li>
<li>The <a href="/wiki/Quran_alone" class="mw-redirect" title="Quran alone">Quranists</a> are Muslims who generally reject the Hadith.</li>
</ul>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Non-denominational_Muslims">Non-denominational Muslims</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Nondenominational_Muslim" class="mw-redirect" title="Nondenominational Muslim">Nondenominational Muslim</a></div>
<p>Non-denominational Muslims is an <a href="/wiki/Umbrella_term" title="Umbrella term">umbrella term</a> that has been used for and by <a href="/wiki/Muslim" title="Muslim">Muslims</a> who do not belong to or do not self-identify with a specific <a href="/wiki/Islamic_denomination" class="mw-redirect" title="Islamic denomination">Islamic denomination</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-306" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-306">[304]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Longton_307-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Longton-307">[305]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Kirkham_308-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kirkham-308">[306]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Pollack_309-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Pollack-309">[307]</a></sup> Prominent figures who refused to identify with a particular Islamic denomination have included <a href="/wiki/Jamal_ad-Din_al-Afghani" class="mw-redirect" title="Jamal ad-Din al-Afghani">Jamal ad-Din al-Afghani</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-310" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-310">[308]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Muhammad_Iqbal" title="Muhammad Iqbal">Muhammad Iqbal</a><sup id="cite_ref-Jones2011_311-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Jones2011-311">[309]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Muhammad_Ali_Jinnah" title="Muhammad Ali Jinnah">Muhammad Ali Jinnah</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-312" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-312">[310]</a></sup> Recent surveys report that large proportions of Muslims in some parts of the world self-identify as "just Muslim", although there is little published analysis available regarding the motivations underlying this response.<sup id="cite_ref-Pew_301-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Pew-301">[299]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-313" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-313">[311]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-314" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-314">[312]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-315" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-315">[313]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Pew_Research_Center" title="Pew Research Center">Pew Research Center</a> reports that respondents self-identifying as "just Muslim" make up a majority of Muslims in seven countries (and a plurality in three others), with the highest proportion in <a href="/wiki/Kazakhstan" title="Kazakhstan">Kazakhstan</a> at 74%. At least one in five Muslims in at least 22 countries self-identify in this way.<sup id="cite_ref-Pew_301-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Pew-301">[299]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Derived_religions">Derived religions</span></h3>
<p>Some movements, such as the <a href="/wiki/Druze" title="Druze">Druze</a>, <a href="/wiki/Berghouata" class="mw-redirect" title="Berghouata">Berghouata</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ha-Mim" title="Ha-Mim">Ha-Mim</a>, either emerged from Islam or came to share certain beliefs with Islam and whether each is separate a religion or a sect of Islam is sometimes controversial. <a href="/wiki/Yazd%C3%A2nism" title="Yazdânism">Yazdânism</a> is seen as a blend of local Kurdish beliefs and Islamic Sufi doctrine introduced to <a href="/wiki/Kurdistan" title="Kurdistan">Kurdistan</a> by <a href="/wiki/Sheikh_Adi_ibn_Musafir" title="Sheikh Adi ibn Musafir">Sheikh Adi ibn Musafir</a> in the 12th century. <a href="/wiki/B%C3%A1bism" title="Bábism">Bábism</a> stems from Twelver Shia passed through <a href="/wiki/Siyyid_%27Ali_Muhammad_i-Shirazi_al-Bab" class="mw-redirect" title="Siyyid 'Ali Muhammad i-Shirazi al-Bab">Siyyid 'Ali Muhammad i-Shirazi al-Bab</a> while one of his followers Mirza Husayn 'Ali Nuri <a href="/wiki/Baha%27u%27llah" class="mw-redirect" title="Baha'u'llah">Baha'u'llah</a> founded the <a href="/wiki/Bahai_Faith" class="mw-redirect" title="Bahai Faith">Bahai Faith</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-316" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-316">[314]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Sikhism" title="Sikhism">Sikhism</a>, founded by <a href="/wiki/Guru_Nanak" title="Guru Nanak">Guru Nanak</a> in late fifteenth century <a href="/wiki/Punjab" title="Punjab">Punjab</a>, incorporates aspects of both Islam and <a href="/wiki/Hinduism" title="Hinduism">Hinduism</a>. African American Muslim movements include the <a href="/wiki/Nation_of_Islam" title="Nation of Islam">Nation of Islam</a>, <a href="/wiki/Five-Percent_Nation" title="Five-Percent Nation">Five-Percent Nation</a> and <a href="/wiki/Moorish_scientists" class="mw-redirect" title="Moorish scientists">Moorish scientists</a>.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Demographics">Demographics</span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Muslim_world" title="Muslim world">Muslim world</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ummah" title="Ummah">Ummah</a></div>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/List_of_countries_by_Muslim_population" class="mw-redirect" title="List of countries by Muslim population">List of countries by Muslim population</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:402px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Islam_percent_population_in_each_nation_World_Map_Muslim_data_by_Pew_Research.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/ba/Islam_percent_population_in_each_nation_World_Map_Muslim_data_by_Pew_Research.svg/400px-Islam_percent_population_in_each_nation_World_Map_Muslim_data_by_Pew_Research.svg.png" width="400" height="205" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/ba/Islam_percent_population_in_each_nation_World_Map_Muslim_data_by_Pew_Research.svg/600px-Islam_percent_population_in_each_nation_World_Map_Muslim_data_by_Pew_Research.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/ba/Islam_percent_population_in_each_nation_World_Map_Muslim_data_by_Pew_Research.svg/800px-Islam_percent_population_in_each_nation_World_Map_Muslim_data_by_Pew_Research.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="863" data-file-height="443" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Islam_percent_population_in_each_nation_World_Map_Muslim_data_by_Pew_Research.svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
World Muslim population by percentage (<a href="/wiki/Pew_Research_Center" title="Pew Research Center">Pew Research Center</a>, 2014).</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>A comprehensive 2009 demographic study of 232 countries and territories reported that 23% of the global population, or 1.57&#160;billion people, are Muslims. Of those, it is estimated that over 75–90% are <a href="/wiki/Sunni" class="mw-redirect" title="Sunni">Sunni</a> and 10–20% are <a href="/wiki/Shia" class="mw-redirect" title="Shia">Shia</a><sup id="cite_ref-mgmpPRC_40-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-mgmpPRC-40">[39]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Britannica_277-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Britannica-277">[275]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-317" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-317">[315]</a></sup> with a small minority belonging to other sects. Approximately 57 countries are <a href="/wiki/List_of_Muslim_majority_countries" class="mw-redirect" title="List of Muslim majority countries">Muslim-majority</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-318" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-318">[316]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Arabs" title="Arabs">Arabs</a> account for around 20% of all Muslims worldwide.<sup id="cite_ref-319" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-319">[317]</a></sup> The number of Muslims worldwide increased from 200 million in 1900 to 551&#160;million in 1970,<sup id="cite_ref-320" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-320">[318]</a></sup> and tripled to 1.6&#160;billion by 2010.<sup id="cite_ref-pew2015_273-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-pew2015-273">[271]</a></sup></p>
<p>The majority of Muslims live in Asia and Africa.<sup id="cite_ref-321" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-321">[319]</a></sup> Approximately 62% of the world's Muslims live in Asia, with over 683&#160;million adherents in <a href="/wiki/Indonesia" title="Indonesia">Indonesia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pakistan" title="Pakistan">Pakistan</a>, <a href="/wiki/India" title="India">India</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Bangladesh" title="Bangladesh">Bangladesh</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-USN.26WR_322-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-USN.26WR-322">[320]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-323" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-323">[321]</a></sup> In the Middle East, non-Arab countries such as <a href="/wiki/Iran" title="Iran">Iran</a> and <a href="/wiki/Turkey" title="Turkey">Turkey</a> are the largest Muslim-majority countries; in Africa, <a href="/wiki/Egypt" title="Egypt">Egypt</a> and <a href="/wiki/Nigeria" title="Nigeria">Nigeria</a> have the most populous Muslim communities.<sup id="cite_ref-Islam_by_country_324-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Islam_by_country-324">[322]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-325" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-325">[323]</a></sup></p>
<p>Most estimates indicate that the People's Republic of China has approximately 20 to 30 million Muslims (1.5% to 2% of the population).<sup id="cite_ref-326" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-326">[324]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-327" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-327">[325]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-328" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-328">[326]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-329" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-329">[327]</a></sup> However, data provided by the <a href="/wiki/San_Diego_State_University" title="San Diego State University">San Diego State University</a>'s International Population Center to <i><a href="/wiki/U.S._News_%26_World_Report" title="U.S. News &amp; World Report">U.S. News &amp; World Report</a></i> suggests that China has 65.3&#160;million Muslims.<sup id="cite_ref-330" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-330">[328]</a></sup> Islam is the second largest religion after Christianity in many European countries,<sup id="cite_ref-331" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-331">[329]</a></sup> and is slowly catching up to that status in the Americas, with between 2,454,000, according to Pew Forum, and approximately 7 million Muslims, according to the Council on American-Islamic Relations (CAIR), in the United States.<sup id="cite_ref-mgmpPRC_40-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-mgmpPRC-40">[39]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-332" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-332">[330]</a></sup></p>
<p>According to the <a href="/wiki/Pew_Research_Center" title="Pew Research Center">Pew Research Center</a>, Islam is set to equal Christianity in number of adherents by the year 2050. Islam is set to grow faster than any other major world religion, reaching a total number of 2.76&#160;billion (an increase of 73%). High fertility rates play a factor, with Islam having a rate of 3.1 compared to the world average of 2.5, and the minimum replacement level for a population at 2.1. Age also plays a role in these numbers due to the fact that Islam has the highest number of adherents under the age of 15 (34% of the total religion) of any major religion (Christianity's is 27%). Sixty percent of Muslims are between the ages of 16 and 59, while only 7% are aged 60+ (the smallest percentage of any major religion). Countries such as Nigeria and the Republic of Macedonia are expected to have Muslim majorities by 2050. In India, the Muslim population will be larger than any other country. Europe's domestic population is set to shrink as opposed to their Islamic population which is set to grow to 10% of Europe's total.<sup id="cite_ref-pew2015_273-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-pew2015-273">[271]</a></sup> According to BBC News, the rates of growth of <a href="/wiki/Islam_in_Europe" title="Islam in Europe">Islam in Europe</a> reveal that the growing number of Muslims is due primarily to immigration and higher <a href="/wiki/Birth_rate" title="Birth rate">birth rates</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-333" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-333">[331]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Culture">Culture</span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Islamic_culture" title="Islamic culture">Islamic culture</a></div>
<p>The term "<a href="/wiki/Islamic_culture" title="Islamic culture">Islamic culture</a>" could be used to mean aspects of culture that pertain to the religion, such as <a href="/wiki/Festivals" class="mw-redirect" title="Festivals">festivals</a> and dress code. It is also controversially used to denote the cultural aspects of traditionally <a href="/wiki/Muslim" title="Muslim">Muslim</a> people.<sup id="cite_ref-334" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-334">[332]</a></sup> Finally, "Islamic civilization" may also refer to the aspects of the synthesized culture of the early Caliphates, including that of non-Muslims,<sup id="cite_ref-335" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-335">[333]</a></sup> sometimes referred to as "<a href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/Islamicate" class="extiw" title="wikt:Islamicate">Islamicate</a>".</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Architecture">Architecture</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Islamic_architecture" title="Islamic architecture">Islamic architecture</a></div>
<p>Perhaps the most important expression of Islamic architecture is that of the mosque.<sup id="cite_ref-336" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-336">[334]</a></sup> Varying cultures have an effect on mosque architecture. For example, North African and Spanish Islamic architecture such as the <a href="/wiki/Mosque_of_Uqba" class="mw-redirect" title="Mosque of Uqba">Great Mosque of Kairouan</a> contain <a href="/wiki/Marble" title="Marble">marble</a> and <a href="/wiki/Porphyry_(geology)" title="Porphyry (geology)">porphyry</a> columns from Roman and Byzantine buildings,<sup id="cite_ref-337" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-337">[335]</a></sup> while <a href="/wiki/Mosques_in_Indonesia" class="mw-redirect" title="Mosques in Indonesia">mosques in Indonesia</a> often have multi-tiered roofs from local <a href="/wiki/Java" title="Java">Javan</a> styles.</p>
<center>

<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Art">Art</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Islamic_art" title="Islamic art">Islamic art</a></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Islamic_art" title="Islamic art">Islamic art</a> encompasses the <a href="/wiki/Visual_arts" title="Visual arts">visual arts</a> produced from the 7th century onwards by people (not necessarily <a href="/wiki/Muslim" title="Muslim">Muslim</a>) who lived within the territory that was inhabited by Muslim populations.<sup id="cite_ref-338" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-338">[336]</a></sup> It includes fields as varied as architecture, <a href="/wiki/Calligraphy" title="Calligraphy">calligraphy</a>, painting, and <a href="/wiki/Ceramics_(art)" class="mw-redirect" title="Ceramics (art)">ceramics</a>, among others.</p>
<p>While not condemned in the Quran, making images of human beings and animals is frowned on in many Islamic cultures and connected with <a href="/wiki/Aniconism_in_Islam" title="Aniconism in Islam">laws against idolatry</a> common to all Abrahamic religions, as 'Abdullaah ibn Mas'ood reported that Muhammad said, "Those who will be most severely punished by Allah on the Day of Resurrection will be the image-makers" (reported by al-Bukhaari, see al-Fath, 10/382). However this rule has been interpreted in different ways by different scholars and in different historical periods, and there are examples of paintings of both animals and humans in Mughal, Persian and Turkish art. The existence of this <a href="/wiki/Aniconism_in_Islam" title="Aniconism in Islam">aversion to creating images of animate beings</a> has been used to explain the prevalence of calligraphy, tessellation and pattern as key aspects of Islamic artistic culture.<sup id="cite_ref-339" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-339">[337]</a></sup></p>
<center>
<ul class="gallery mw-gallery-traditional">
<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 275px">
<div style="width: 275px">
<div class="thumb" style="width: 270px;">
